this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Hellenistic period</b> covers the period of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean history</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Death of Alexander the Great">death</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> in 323 BC and the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> as signified by the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a> in 31 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and the subsequent conquest of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word Hellas (&#7961;&#955;&#955;&#940;&#962;, Ell&#225;s) is the original word for Greece, from which the word <i>Hellenistic</i> was derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At this time, Greek cultural influence and power was at its peak in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, experiencing prosperity and progress in the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_theatre" title="Ancient Greek theatre">theatre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_mathematics" title="Hellenistic mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">philosophy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_science" title="Hellenistic science">science</a>. It is often considered a period of transition, sometimes even of <a href="/wiki/Decadence" title="Decadence">decadence</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneration" title="Degeneration">degeneration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">[4]</a></sup> compared to the enlightenment of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical era</a>. The Hellenistic period saw the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Comedy" title="New Comedy">New Comedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_school" title="Alexandrian school">Alexandrian poetry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> and the philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a>. Greek science was advanced by the works of the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> and the polymath <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>. The religious sphere expanded to include new gods such as the Greco-Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Serapis" title="Serapis">Serapis</a>, eastern deities such as <a href="/wiki/Attis" title="Attis">Attis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cybele" title="Cybele">Cybele</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">syncretism between Hellenistic culture and Buddhism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and Northwest <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yale_Art_Gallery,_First_Floor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7360" data-file-width="4912" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Yale_Art_Gallery%2C_First_Floor.jpg/220px-Yale_Art_Gallery%2C_First_Floor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Yale_Art_Gallery%2C_First_Floor.jpg/330px-Yale_Art_Gallery%2C_First_Floor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Yale_Art_Gallery%2C_First_Floor.jpg/440px-Yale_Art_Gallery%2C_First_Floor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yale_Art_Gallery,_First_Floor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hellenistic period. Dionysus sculpture from the Ancient Art Collection at Yale.</div></div></div>\n<p>After <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>\'s invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> in 330 BC and its disintegration shortly after, the Hellenistic kingdoms were established throughout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_Asia" title="Southwest Asia">south-west Asia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attalid_dynasty" title="Attalid dynasty">Kingdom of Pergamon</a>), north-east Africa (<a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a>) and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>). The Hellenistic period was characterized by a new wave of Greek colonization<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> which established Greek cities and kingdoms in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilcken_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilcken-6">[6]</a></sup> This resulted in the export of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_culture" title="Greek culture">Greek culture</a> and language to these new realms, spanning as far as modern-day India. Equally, however, these new kingdoms were influenced by the indigenous cultures, adopting local practices where beneficial, necessary, or convenient. Hellenistic culture thus represents a fusion of the Ancient Greek world with that of the Near East, Middle East, and Southwest Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> This mixture gave rise to a common <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic</a>-based Greek dialect, known as <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, which became the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> through the Hellenistic world.\n</p><p>Scholars and historians are divided as to what event signals the end of the Hellenistic era. The Hellenistic period may be seen to end either with the final conquest of the Greek heartlands by <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Rome</a> in 146 BC following the <a href="/wiki/Achaean_War" title="Achaean War">Achean War</a>, with the final defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a> in 31&#160;BC, or even the move by <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> of the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 330 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-9">[9]</a></sup> "Hellenistic" is distinguished from "Hellenic" in that the first encompasses the entire sphere of direct ancient Greek influence, while the latter refers to <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Greece itself</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Hellenization_and_acculturation"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Hellenization and acculturation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">Names of the Greeks</a></div>\n<p>The word originated from the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> term <i>hellenistisch</i>, from Ancient Greek &#7961;&#955;&#955;&#951;&#957;&#953;&#963;&#964;&#942;&#962; (<i>Hell&#275;nist&#7703;s</i>, "one who uses the Greek language"), from &#7961;&#955;&#955;&#940;&#962; (<i>Hell&#225;s</i>, "Greece"); as if "Hellenist" + "ic".\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:298px;max-width:298px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:112px;max-width:112px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UrumqiWarrior.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="445" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/110px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/165px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/220px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BactrianZoroastrian.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="475" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg/180px-BactrianZoroastrian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg/270px-BactrianZoroastrian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg/360px-BactrianZoroastrian.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Sampul_tapestry" title="Sampul tapestry">Sampul tapestry</a>, a woolen wall hanging from <a href="/wiki/Lop_County" title="Lop County">Lop County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, showing a possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Greek soldier</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a> (250-125 BC), with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" title="Blue eyes">blue eyes</a>, wielding a spear, and wearing what appears to be a <i><a href="/wiki/Diadem" title="Diadem">diadem</a></i> headband; depicted above him is a <a href="/wiki/Centaur" title="Centaur">centaur</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, a common <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motif</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a><br/> <b>Right image</b>: painted clay and <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> head of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> priest wearing a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a>-style headdress, <a href="/wiki/Takhti-Sangin" title="Takhti-Sangin">Takhti-Sangin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, 3rd-2nd century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>"Hellenistic" is a modern word and a 19th-century concept; the idea of a Hellenistic period did not exist in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>. Although words related in form or meaning, e.g. <i>Hellenist</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">&#7961;&#955;&#955;&#951;&#957;&#953;&#963;&#964;&#942;&#962;</span>, <i>Hell&#275;nist&#275;s</i>), have been attested since ancient times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HellenistesLSJ_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-HellenistesLSJ-10">[10]</a></sup> it was <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gustav_Droysen" title="Johann Gustav Droysen">Johann Gustav Droysen</a> in the mid-19th century, who in his classic work <i>Geschichte des Hellenismus</i> (<i>History of Hellenism</i>), coined the term <i>Hellenistic</i> to refer to and define the period when Greek culture spread in the non-Greek world after Alexander\'s conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DroysenHellenismus_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-DroysenHellenismus-11">[11]</a></sup> Following Droysen, <i>Hellenistic</i> and related terms, e.g. <i>Hellenism</i>, have been widely used in various contexts; a notable such use is in <i><a href="/wiki/Culture_and_Anarchy" title="Culture and Anarchy">Culture and Anarchy</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>, where Hellenism is used in contrast with <a href="/wiki/Hebraism" title="Hebraism">Hebraism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MatthewArnold_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-MatthewArnold-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The major issue with the term Hellenistic lies in its convenience, as the spread of Greek culture was not the generalized phenomenon that the term implies. Some areas of the conquered world were more affected by Greek influences than others. The term Hellenistic also implies that the Greek populations were of majority in the areas in which they settled, but in many cases, the Greek settlers were actually the minority among the native populations. The Greek population and the native population did not always mix; the Greeks moved and brought their own culture, but interaction did not always occur.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>While a few fragments exist, there is no complete surviving historical work which dates to the hundred years following Alexander\'s death. The works of the major Hellenistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historians" title="Historians">historians</a> <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_of_Cardia" title="Hieronymus of Cardia">Hieronymus of Cardia</a> (who worked under Alexander, Antigonus I and other successors), <a href="/wiki/Duris_of_Samos" title="Duris of Samos">Duris of Samos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phylarchus" title="Phylarchus">Phylarchus</a> which were used by surviving <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">sources</a> are all lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbank_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walbank-13">[13]</a></sup> The earliest and most credible surviving source for the Hellenistic period is <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> of Megalopolis (c. 200&#8211;118), a statesman of the <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">Achaean League</a> until 168 BC when he was forced to go to Rome as a hostage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbank_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walbank-13">[13]</a></sup> His <i><a href="/wiki/The_Histories_(Polybius)" title="The Histories (Polybius)">Histories</a></i> eventually grew to a length of forty books, covering the years 220 to 167 BC.\n</p><p>The most important source after Polybius is <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> who wrote his <i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">Bibliotheca historica</a></i> between 60 and 30 BC and reproduced some important earlier sources such as Hieronymus, but his account of the Hellenistic period breaks off after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ipsus" title="Battle of Ipsus">battle of Ipsus</a> (301). Another important source, <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>\'s (c. 50&#8211;c. 120) <i><a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">Parallel Lives</a></i> although more preoccupied with issues of personal character and morality, outlines the history of important Hellenistic figures. <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a> of Alexandria (late 1st century AD&#8211;before 165) wrote a history of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_empire" title="Roman empire">Roman empire</a> that includes information of some Hellenistic kingdoms.\n</p><p>Other sources include <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a>\'s (2nd century AD) <a href="/wiki/Epitome" title="Epitome">epitome</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompeius_Trogus" title="Pompeius Trogus">Pompeius Trogus</a>\' <i>Historiae Philipicae</i> and a summary of <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a>\'s <i>Events after Alexander</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios I of Constantinople</a>. Lesser supplementary sources include <a href="/wiki/Curtius_Rufus" title="Curtius Rufus">Curtius Rufus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pausanias_of_Damascus" title="Pausanias of Damascus">Pausanias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">encyclopedia</a> the <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a>. In the field of philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes La&#235;rtius">Diogenes La&#235;rtius</a>\' <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_and_Opinions_of_Eminent_Philosophers" title="Lives and Opinions of Eminent Philosophers">Lives and Opinions of Eminent Philosophers</a></i> is the main source; works such as <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_Natura_Deorum" title="De Natura Deorum">De Natura Deorum</a></i> also provide some further detail of philosophical schools in the Hellenistic period.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2766" data-file-width="3905" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg/220px-Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg/330px-Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg/440px-Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Bucephalus_-_Battle_of_Issus_mosaic_-_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_-_Naples_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a> fighting the Persian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III</a>. From the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mosaic" title="Alexander Mosaic">Alexander Mosaic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naples_National_Archaeological_Museum" title="Naples National Archaeological Museum">Naples National Archaeological Museum</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">Wars of Alexander the Great</a></div>\n<p>Ancient Greece had traditionally been a fractious collection of fiercely independent city-states. After the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> (431&#8211;404 BC), Greece had fallen under a <a href="/wiki/Spartan_hegemony" title="Spartan hegemony">Spartan hegemony</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> was pre-eminent but not all-powerful. Spartan hegemony was succeeded by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thebes_(Greece)" title="Thebes (Greece)">Theban</a> <a href="/wiki/Theban_hegemony" title="Theban hegemony">one</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leuctra" title="Battle of Leuctra">Battle of Leuctra</a> (371 BC), but after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mantinea_(362_BC)" title="Battle of Mantinea (362 BC)">Battle of Mantinea (362 BC)</a>, all of Greece was so weakened that no one state could claim pre-eminence. It was against this backdrop that the ascendancy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> began, under king <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a>. Macedon was located at the periphery of the Greek world, and although its royal family claimed Greek descent, the Macedonians themselves were looked down upon as semi-barbaric by the rest of the Greeks. However, Macedon had a relatively strong and centralised government, and compared to most Greek states, directly controlled a large area.\n</p><p>Philip II was a strong and expansionist king and he took every opportunity to expand Macedonian territory. In 352 BC he annexed <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_Prefecture" title="Magnesia Prefecture">Magnesia</a>. In 338 BC, Philip defeated a combined Theban and Athenian army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaeronea_(338_BC)" title="Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)">Battle of Chaeronea</a> after a decade of desultory conflict. In the aftermath, Philip formed the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">League of Corinth</a>, effectively bringing the majority of Greece under his direct sway. He was elected <i>Hegemon</i> of the league, and a campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> of Persia was planned. However, while this campaign was in its early stages, he was assassinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MacedonEmpire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="951" data-file-width="1990" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/220px-MacedonEmpire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/330px-MacedonEmpire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/440px-MacedonEmpire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MacedonEmpire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander\'s empire at the time of its maximum expansion.</div></div></div>\n<p>Succeeding his father, Alexander took over the Persian war himself. During a decade of campaigning, Alexander <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">conquered the whole Persian Empire</a>, overthrowing the Persian king <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>. The conquered lands included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Media</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, and parts of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steppes" title="Steppes">steppes</a> of central Asia. The years of constant campaigning had taken their toll however, and Alexander died in 323 BC.\n</p><p>After his death, the huge territories Alexander had conquered became subject to a strong Greek influence (<a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a>) for the next two or three centuries, until the rise of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Rome</a> in the west, and of <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthia</a> in the east. As the Greek and Levantine cultures mingled, the development of a hybrid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilisation" title="Hellenistic civilisation">Hellenistic culture</a> began, and persisted even when isolated from the main centres of Greek culture (for instance, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a>).\n</p><p>It can be argued that some of the changes across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> after Alexander\'s conquests and during the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a> would have occurred without the influence of Greek rule. As mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(historian)" title="Peter Green (historian)">Peter Green</a>, numerous factors of conquest have been merged under the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Period" title="Hellenistic Period">Hellenistic Period</a>. Specific areas conquered by Alexander\'s invading army, including Egypt and areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> "fell" willingly to conquest and viewed Alexander as more of a liberator than a conqueror.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition, much of the area conquered would continue to be ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>, Alexander\'s generals and successors. Initially the whole empire was divided among them; however, some territories were lost relatively quickly, or only remained nominally under Macedonian rule. After 200 years, only much reduced and rather degenerate states remained,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-9">[9]</a></sup> until the conquest of Ptolemaic Egypt by Rome.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Diadochi">The Diadochi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Diadochi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">Wars of the Diadochi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">Partition of Babylon</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="History of Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">History of Macedonia (ancient kingdom)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1353" data-file-width="2828" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png/250px-Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png/375px-Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png/500px-Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diadochi_satraps_babylon.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The distribution of <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrapies</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">Settlement in Babylon (323 BC)</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>When Alexander the Great died (10 June 323 BC), he left behind a huge empire which was composed of many essentially autonomous territories called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satrapies" title="Satrapies">satrapies</a>. Without a chosen successor there were immediate disputes among his generals as to who should be king of Macedon. These generals became known as the Diadochi (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#916;&#953;&#940;&#948;&#959;&#967;&#959;&#953;</span>, <i>Diadokhoi</i>, meaning "Successors").\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Meleager_(general)" title="Meleager (general)">Meleager</a> and the infantry supported the candidacy of Alexander\'s half-brother, <a href="/wiki/Philip_III_of_Macedon" title="Philip III of Macedon">Philip Arrhidaeus</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a>, the leading cavalry commander, supported waiting until the birth of Alexander\'s child by <a href="/wiki/Roxana" title="Roxana">Roxana</a>. After the infantry stormed the palace of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, a compromise was arranged &#8211; Arrhidaeus (as Philip III) should become king, and should rule jointly with Roxana\'s child, assuming that it was a boy (as it was, becoming <a href="/wiki/Alexander_IV_of_Macedon" title="Alexander IV of Macedon">Alexander IV</a>). Perdiccas himself would become regent (<i>epimeletes</i>) of the empire, and Meleager his lieutenant. Soon, however, Perdiccas had Meleager and the other infantry leaders murdered, and assumed full control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The generals who had supported Perdiccas were rewarded in the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">partition of Babylon</a> by becoming <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satraps</a> of the various parts of the empire, but Perdiccas\' position was shaky, because, as Arrian writes, "everyone was suspicious of him, and he of them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diadochi_wars" title="Diadochi wars">Diadochi wars</a> broke out when <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> planned to marry Alexander\'s sister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_of_Macedonia" title="Cleopatra of Macedonia">Cleopatra</a> and began to question <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_I_Monophthalmus" title="Antigonus I Monophthalmus">Antigonus I Monophthalmus</a>\' leadership in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. Antigonus fled for Greece, and then, together with <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a> (the satrap of <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> who had been in Greece fighting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamian_war" title="Lamian war">Lamian war</a>) invaded <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. The rebels were supported by <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a>, the satrap of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and Ptolemy, the satrap of Egypt. Although <a href="/wiki/Eumenes" title="Eumenes">Eumenes</a>, satrap of <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, defeated the rebels in Asia Minor, Perdiccas himself was murdered by his own generals Peithon, <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antigenes_(general)" title="Antigenes (general)">Antigenes</a> (possibly with Ptolemy\'s aid) during his invasion of Egypt (c. 21 May to 19 June, 320 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Ptolemy came to terms with Perdiccas\'s murderers, making Peithon and <a href="/wiki/Arrhidaeus" title="Arrhidaeus">Arrhidaeus</a> regents in his place, but soon these came to a new agreement with Antipater at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Triparadisus" title="Treaty of Triparadisus">Treaty of Triparadisus</a>. Antipater was made regent of the Empire, and the two kings were moved to Macedon. Antigonus remained in charge of Asia Minor, Ptolemy retained Egypt, Lysimachus retained Thrace and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucus_I" title="Seleucus I">Seleucus I</a> controlled <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>.\n</p><p>The second Diadochi war began following the death of <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a> in 319 BC. Passing over his own son, <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a>, Antipater had declared <a href="/wiki/Polyperchon" title="Polyperchon">Polyperchon</a> his successor as Regent. Cassander rose in revolt against Polyperchon (who was joined by Eumenes) and was supported by Antigonus, Lysimachus and Ptolemy. In 317, <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a> invaded Macedonia, attaining control of Macedon, sentencing <a href="/wiki/Olympias" title="Olympias">Olympias</a> to death and capturing the boy king <a href="/wiki/Alexander_IV_of_Macedon" title="Alexander IV of Macedon">Alexander IV</a>, and his mother. In Asia, <a href="/wiki/Eumenes" title="Eumenes">Eumenes</a> was betrayed by his own men after years of campaign and was given up to Antigonus who had him executed.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diadoch.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="1752" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Diadoch.png/250px-Diadoch.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Diadoch.png/375px-Diadoch.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Diadoch.png/500px-Diadoch.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diadoch.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Kingdoms of Antigonos and his rivals c. 303 BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>The third war of the Diadochi broke out because of the growing power and ambition of Antigonus. He began removing and appointing satraps as if he were king and also raided the royal treasuries in Ecbatana, Persepolis and Susa, making off with 25,000 <a href="/wiki/Talent_(measurement)" title="Talent (measurement)">talents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Seleucus was forced to flee to Egypt and Antigonus was soon at war with Ptolemy, Lysimachus, and Cassander. He then invaded <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>, laid siege to <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, stormed <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a> and began building a fleet. Ptolemy invaded Syria and defeated Antigonus\' son, <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_of_Macedon" title="Demetrius I of Macedon">Demetrius Poliorcetes</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaza_(312_BC)" title="Battle of Gaza (312 BC)">Battle of Gaza</a> of 312 BC which allowed <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus</a> to secure control of <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, and the eastern satrapies. In 310, Cassander had young King Alexander IV and his mother Roxane murdered, ending the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argead_Dynasty" title="Argead Dynasty">Argead Dynasty</a> which had ruled Macedon for several centuries.\n</p><p>Antigonus then sent his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_Poliorcetes" title="Demetrius I Poliorcetes">Demetrius</a> to regain control of Greece. In 307 he took Athens, expelling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_of_Phaleron" title="Demetrius of Phaleron">Demetrius of Phaleron</a>, Cassander\'s governor, and proclaiming the city free again. Demetrius now turned his attention to Ptolemy, defeating his fleet at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salamis_in_Cyprus_(306_BC)" title="Battle of Salamis in Cyprus (306 BC)">Battle of Salamis</a> and taking control of Cyprus. In the aftermath of this victory, Antigonus took the title of king (<i><a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a></i>) and bestowed it on his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_Poliorcetes" title="Demetrius Poliorcetes">Demetrius Poliorcetes</a>, the rest of the Diadochi soon followed suit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Demetrius continued his campaigns by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Rhodes_(305_BC)" title="Siege of Rhodes (305 BC)">laying siege to Rhodes</a> and conquering most of Greece in 302, creating a league against Cassander\'s Macedon.\n</p><p>The decisive engagement of the war came when Lysimachus invaded and overran much of western Anatolia, but was soon isolated by Antigonus and Demetrius near Ipsus in <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a>. Seleucus arrived in time to save Lysimachus and utterly crushed Antigonus at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ipsus" title="Battle of Ipsus">Battle of Ipsus</a> in 301 BC. Seleucus\' war elephants proved decisive, Antigonus was killed, and Demetrius fled back to Greece to attempt to preserve the remnants of his rule there by recapturing a rebellious Athens. Meanwhile, Lysimachus took over <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, Seleucus took <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, and Ptolemy captured <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="961" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/220px-Diadochen1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/330px-Diadochen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/440px-Diadochen1.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kingdoms of the Diadochi after the battle of Ipsus, c. 301 BC. <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #787CAD; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy I Soter</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #50A249; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #C38833; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #C3B933; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>After Cassander\'s death in 298 BC, however, Demetrius, who still maintained a sizable loyal army and fleet, invaded Macedon, seized the Macedonian throne (294) and conquered <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> and most of central Greece (293&#8211;291).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> He was defeated in 288 BC when <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a> <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">of Thrace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus of Epirus</a> invaded Macedon on two fronts, and quickly carved up the kingdom for themselves. Demetrius fled to central Greece with his mercenaries and began to build support there and in the northern Peloponnese. He once again laid siege to Athens after they turned on him, but then struck a treaty with the Athenians and Ptolemy, which allowed him to cross over to Asia Minor and wage war on Lysimachus\' holdings in <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, leaving his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigonus_Gonatas" title="Antigonus Gonatas">Antigonus Gonatas</a> in Greece. After initial successes, he was forced to surrender to Seleucus in 285 and later died in captivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Lysimachus, who had seized Macedon and Thessaly for himself, was forced into war when Seleucus invaded his territories in Asia minor and was defeated and killed in 281 BC at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Corupedium" title="Battle of Corupedium">Battle of Corupedium</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>. Seleucus then attempted to conquer Lysimachus\' European territories in Thrace and Macedon, but he was assassinated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_Ceraunus" title="Ptolemy Ceraunus">Ptolemy Ceraunus</a> ("the thunderbolt"), who had taken refuge at the Seleucid court and then had himself acclaimed as king of Macedon. Ptolemy was killed when Macedon was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_settlement_of_Eastern_Europe#Invasions_of_Greece" title="Celtic settlement of Eastern Europe">invaded by Gauls in 279</a>&#8212;his head stuck on a spear&#8212;and the country fell into anarchy. Antigonus II Gonatas invaded Thrace in the summer of 277 and defeated a large force of 18,000 Gauls. He was quickly hailed as king of Macedon and went on to rule for 35 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At this point the tripartite territorial division of the Hellenistic age was in place, with the main Hellenistic powers being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> under Demetrius\'s son <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_II_Gonatas" title="Antigonus II Gonatas">Antigonus II Gonatas</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_kingdom" title="Ptolemaic kingdom">Ptolemaic kingdom</a> under the aged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I" title="Ptolemy I">Ptolemy I</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid_empire" title="Seleucid empire">Seleucid empire</a> under Seleucus\' son <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_I_Soter" title="Antiochus I Soter">Antiochus I Soter</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Europe">Southern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Southern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Epirus">Kingdom of Epirus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Epirus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epirus_(ancient_state)" title="Epirus (ancient state)">Epirus (ancient state)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="346" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif/150px-Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif/225px-Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif/300px-Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pyrrhus_and_his_Elephants.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pyrrhus and his elephants.</div></div></div>\n<p>Epirus was a <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek#Northwest_Greek" title="Doric Greek">northwestern Greek</a> kingdom in the western <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> ruled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molossian" title="Molossian">Molossian</a> <a href="/wiki/Aeacidae" title="Aeacidae">Aeacidae</a> dynasty. Epirus was an ally of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> during the reigns of Philip II and Alexander.\n</p><p>In 281 <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus</a> (nicknamed "the eagle", <i>aetos</i>) invaded southern Italy to aid the city state of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taranto" title="History of Taranto">Tarentum</a>. Pyrrhus defeated the Romans in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Heraclea" title="Battle of Heraclea">Battle of Heraclea</a> and at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Asculum" title="Battle of Asculum">Battle of Asculum</a>. Though victorious, he was forced to retreat due to heavy losses, hence the term "<a href="/wiki/Pyrrhic_victory" title="Pyrrhic victory">Pyrrhic victory</a>". Pyrrhus then turned south and invaded Sicily but was unsuccessful and returned to Italy. After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Beneventum_(275_BC)" title="Battle of Beneventum (275 BC)">Battle of Beneventum</a> (275 BC) Pyrrhus lost all his Italian holdings and left for Epirus.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus</a> then went to war with Macedonia in 275, deposing <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_II_Gonatas" title="Antigonus II Gonatas">Antigonus II Gonatas</a> and briefly ruling over Macedonia and Thessaly until 285. Afterwards he invaded southern Greece, and was killed in battle against <a href="/wiki/Argos" title="Argos">Argos</a> in 272 BC. After the death of Pyrrhus, Epirus remained a minor power. In 233 BC the Aeacid royal family was deposed and a federal state was set up called the <a href="/wiki/Epirote_League" title="Epirote League">Epirote League</a>. The league was conquered by Rome in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Macedonian_War" title="Third Macedonian War">Third Macedonian War</a> (171&#8211;168 BC).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Macedon">Kingdom of Macedon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Macedon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antigonid_dynasty" title="Antigonid dynasty">Antigonid dynasty</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philip_V_of_Macedon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="298" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Philip_V_of_Macedon.jpg/220px-Philip_V_of_Macedon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Philip_V_of_Macedon.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philip_V_of_Macedon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V</a>, "<i>the darling of Hellas</i>", wearing the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">royal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diadem_(personal_wear)" title="Diadem (personal wear)">diadem</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigonus_II" title="Antigonus II">Antigonus II</a>, a student of <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno of Citium</a>, spent most of his rule defending Macedon against <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> and cementing Macedonian power in Greece, first against the Athenians in the <a href="/wiki/Chremonidean_War" title="Chremonidean War">Chremonidean War</a>, and then against the <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">Achaean League</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aratus_of_Sicyon" title="Aratus of Sicyon">Aratus of Sicyon</a>. Under the Antigonids, Macedonia was often short on funds, the <a href="/wiki/Pangaion_Hills" title="Pangaion Hills">Pangaeum mines</a> were no longer as productive as under Philip II, the wealth from Alexander\'s campaigns had been used up and the countryside pillaged by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_settlement_of_Eastern_Europe#Invasions_of_Greece" title="Celtic settlement of Eastern Europe">Gallic invasion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> A large number of the Macedonian population had also been resettled abroad by Alexander or had chosen to emigrate to the new eastern Greek cities. Up to two thirds of the population emigrated, and the Macedonian army could only count on a levy of 25,000 men, a significantly smaller force than under Philip II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antigonus II ruled until his death in 239 BC. His son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_II_of_Macedon" title="Demetrius II of Macedon">Demetrius II</a> soon died in 229 BC, leaving a child (Philip V) as king, with the general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigonus_Doson" title="Antigonus Doson">Antigonus Doson</a> as regent. Doson led Macedon to victory in the war against the Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Cleomenes_III" title="Cleomenes III">Cleomenes III</a>, and occupied <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V</a>, who came to power when Doson died in 221 BC, was the last Macedonian ruler with both the talent and the opportunity to unite Greece and preserve its independence against the "cloud rising in the west": the ever-increasing power of Rome. He was known as "the darling of Hellas". Under his auspices the Peace of Naupactus (217 BC) brought the latest war between Macedon and the Greek leagues (<a href="/wiki/Social_War_(220%E2%80%93217_BC)" title="Social War (220&#8211;217 BC)">the social war</a> 220-217) to an end, and at this time he controlled all of Greece except Athens, Rhodes and Pergamum.\n</p><p>In 215 BC Philip, with his eye on <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a>, formed an alliance with Rome\'s enemy <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, which led to Roman alliances with the <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">Achaean League</a>, Rhodes and Pergamum. The <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">First Macedonian War</a> broke out in 212 BC, and ended inconclusively in 205 BC. Philip continued to wage war against Pergamum and Rhodes for control of the Aegean (204-200 BC) and ignored Roman demands for non-intervention in Greece by invading Attica. In 198&#160;BC, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Macedonian_War" title="Second Macedonian War">Second Macedonian War</a> Philip was decisively defeated at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cynoscephalae" title="Battle of Cynoscephalae">Cynoscephalae</a> by the Roman proconsul <a href="/wiki/Titus_Quinctius_Flamininus" title="Titus Quinctius Flamininus">Titus Quinctius Flamininus</a> and Macedon lost all its territories in Greece proper. Southern Greece was now thoroughly brought into the Roman <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>, though it retained nominal autonomy. The end of Antigonid Macedon came when Philip V\'s son, Perseus, was defeated and captured by the Romans in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Macedonian_War" title="Third Macedonian War">Third Macedonian War</a> (171&#8211;168 BC).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rest_of_Greece">Rest of Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rest of Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic Greece</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png/250px-Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png/375px-Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png/500px-Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macedonia_and_the_Aegean_World_c.200.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greece and the Aegean World c. 200 BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the Hellenistic period the importance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_peninsula" title="Greek peninsula">Greece proper</a> within the Greek-speaking world declined sharply. The great centers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic culture</a> were <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, capitals of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid_dynasty" title="Seleucid dynasty">Seleucid Syria</a> respectively. The conquests of Alexander greatly widened the horizons of the Greek world, making the endless conflicts between the cities which had marked the 5th and 4th centuries BC seem petty and unimportant. It led to a steady emigration, particularly of the young and ambitious, to the new Greek empires in the east. Many Greeks migrated to Alexandria, Antioch and the many other new Hellenistic cities founded in Alexander\'s wake, as far away as modern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.\n</p><p>Independent city states were unable to compete with Hellenistic kingdoms and were usually forced to ally themselves to one of them for defense, giving honors to Hellenistic rulers in return for protection. One example is <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, which had been decisively defeated by Antipater in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamian_war" title="Lamian war">Lamian war</a> (323-322) and had its port in the <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a> garrisoned by Macedonian troops who supported a conservative <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> After <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_Poliorcetes" title="Demetrius Poliorcetes">Demetrius Poliorcetes</a> captured Athens in 307 and restored the <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">democracy</a>, the Athenians honored him and his father Antigonus by placing gold statues of them on the <a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">agora</a> and granting them the title of king. Athens later allied itself to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> to throw off Macedonian rule, eventually setting up a religious cult for the Ptolemaic kings and naming one of the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Phyle" title="Phyle">phyles</a> in honour of Ptolemy for his aid against Macedon. In spite of the Ptolemaic monies and fleets backing their endeavors, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> were defeated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigonus_II" title="Antigonus II">Antigonus II</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Chremonidean_War" title="Chremonidean War">Chremonidean War</a> (267-261). Athens was then occupied by Macedonian troops, and run by Macedonian officials.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> remained independent, but it was no longer the leading military power in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>. The Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Cleomenes_III" title="Cleomenes III">Cleomenes III</a> (235&#8211;222 BC) staged a military coup against the conservative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephors" title="Ephors">ephors</a> and pushed through radical social and land reforms in order to increase the size of the shrinking Spartan citizenry able to provide military service and restore Spartan power. Sparta\'s bid for supremacy was crushed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sellasia" title="Battle of Sellasia">Battle of Sellasia</a> (222) by the Achaean league and Macedon, who restored the power of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephors" title="Ephors">ephors</a>.\n</p><p>Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_states" title="City states">city states</a> formed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federated_states" title="Federated states">federated states</a> in self-defense, such as the <a href="/wiki/Aetolian_League" title="Aetolian League">Aetolian League</a> (est. 370 BC), the <a href="/wiki/Achaean_League" title="Achaean League">Achaean League</a> (est. 280 BC), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boeotian_league" title="Boeotian league">Boeotian league</a>, the "Northern League" (<a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chalcedon" title="Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heraclea_Pontica" title="Heraclea Pontica">Heraclea Pontica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tium" title="Tium">Tium</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nesiotic_League" title="Nesiotic League">Nesiotic League</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/Cyclades" title="Cyclades">Cyclades</a>. These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federations" title="Federations">federations</a> involved a central government which controlled <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy" title="Foreign policy">foreign policy</a> and military affairs, while leaving most of the local governing to the city states, a system termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sympoliteia_(treaty)" title="Sympoliteia (treaty)">sympoliteia</a>. In states such as the Achaean league, this also involved the admission of other ethnic groups into the federation with equal rights, in this case, non-<a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(tribe)" title="Achaeans (tribe)">Achaeans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Achean league was able to drive out the Macedonians from the Peloponnese and free Corinth, which duly joined the league. \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colosse_de_Rhodes_(Barclay).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Colosse_de_Rhodes_%28Barclay%29.jpg/180px-Colosse_de_Rhodes_%28Barclay%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Colosse_de_Rhodes_%28Barclay%29.jpg/270px-Colosse_de_Rhodes_%28Barclay%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Colosse_de_Rhodes_%28Barclay%29.jpg/360px-Colosse_de_Rhodes_%28Barclay%29.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colosse_de_Rhodes_(Barclay).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Colossus_of_Rhodes" title="Colossus of Rhodes">Colossus of Rhodes</a>, one of the seven wonders of the ancient world.</div></div></div>\n<p>One of the few city states who managed to maintain full independence from the control of any Hellenistic kingdom was <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>. With a skilled navy to protect its trade fleets from pirates and an ideal strategic position covering the routes from the east into the Aegean, Rhodes prospered during the Hellenistic period. It became a center of culture and commerce, its coins were widely circulated and its philosophical schools became one of the best in the Mediterranean. After holding out for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Rhodes_(305_BC)" title="Siege of Rhodes (305 BC)">one year under siege by Demetrius Poliorcetes</a> (305&#8211;304 BC), the Rhodians built the <a href="/wiki/Colossus_of_Rhodes" title="Colossus of Rhodes">Colossus of Rhodes</a> to commemorate their victory. They retained their independence by the maintenance of a powerful navy, by maintaining a carefully neutral posture and acting to preserve the balance of power between the major Hellenistic kingdoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Initially Rhodes had very close ties with the Ptolemaic kingdom. Rhodes later became a Roman ally against the Seleucids, receiving some territory in <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a> for their role in the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Seleucid_War" title="Roman&#8211;Seleucid War">Roman&#8211;Seleucid War</a> (192&#8211;188 BC). Rome eventually turned on Rhodes and annexed the island as a Roman province.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Balkans">Balkans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Balkans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg/220px-Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg/330px-Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg/440px-Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of a groom and bride from the Hellenistic <a href="/wiki/Thracian_Tomb_of_Kazanlak" title="Thracian Tomb of Kazanlak">Thracian Tomb of Kazanlak</a>, near the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Seuthopolis" title="Seuthopolis">Seuthopolis</a>, 4th century BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>The west <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> coast was inhabited by various <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_tribes_in_Illyria" title="List of ancient tribes in Illyria">Illyrian</a> tribes and kingdoms such as the kingdom of the <a href="/wiki/Dalmatae" title="Dalmatae">Dalmatae</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Ardiaei" title="Ardiaei">Ardiaei</a>, who often engaged in <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Teuta" title="Queen Teuta">Queen Teuta</a> (reigned 231 BC to 227 BC). Further inland was the Illyrian <a href="/wiki/Paeonia_(kingdom)" title="Paeonia (kingdom)">Paeonian Kingdom</a> and the tribe of the <a href="/wiki/Agrianes" title="Agrianes">Agrianes</a>. Illyrians on the coast of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adriatic" title="Adriatic">Adriatic</a> were under the effects and influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenisation" title="Hellenisation">Hellenisation</a> and some tribes adopted Greek, becoming bilingual<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalmatia_2006,_page_21_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dalmatia_2006,_page_21-31">[31]</a></sup> due to their proximity to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_cities_in_Illyria#Greek" title="List of ancient cities in Illyria">Greek colonies</a> in Illyria. Illyrians imported weapons and armor from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" title="Ancient Greeks">Ancient Greeks</a> (such as the <a href="/wiki/Illyrian_type_helmet" title="Illyrian type helmet">Illyrian type helmet</a>, originally a Greek type) and also adopted the ornamentation of Ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> on their shields<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and their war belts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> (a single one has been found, dated 3rd century BC at modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selce_e_Poshtme" title="Selce e Poshtme">Selce e Poshtme</a>, a part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a> at the time under <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V of Macedon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>).\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odrysian_Kingdom" title="Odrysian Kingdom">Odrysian Kingdom</a> was a union of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thracian" title="Thracian">Thracian</a> tribes under the kings of the powerful Odrysian tribe centered around the region of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>. Various parts of Thrace were under Macedonian rule under <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II" title="Ptolemy II">Ptolemy II</a>, and Philip V but were also often ruled by their own kings. The <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agrianes" title="Agrianes">Agrianes</a> were widely used by Alexander as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peltasts" title="Peltasts">peltasts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a>, forming about one fifth of his army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The Diadochi also used Thracian mercenaries in their armies and they were also used as colonists. The Odrysians used <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> as the language of administration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and of the nobility. The nobility also adopted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_clothing" title="Greek clothing">Greek fashions in dress</a>, ornament and military equipment, spreading it to the other tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Thracian kings were among the first to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenized" title="Hellenized">Hellenized</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After 278 BC the Odrysians had a strong competitor in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_settlement_of_Eastern_Europe" title="Celtic settlement of Eastern Europe">Celtic</a> <a href="/wiki/Tylis" title="Tylis">Kingdom of Tylis</a> ruled by the kings <a href="/wiki/Comontorius" title="Comontorius">Comontorius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cavarus" title="Cavarus">Cavarus</a>, but in 212 BC they conquered their enemies and destroyed their capital.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Mediterranean">Western Mediterranean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Western Mediterranean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">Colonies in antiquity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_coinage_of_Italy_and_Sicily" title="Greek coinage of Italy and Sicily">Greek coinage of Italy and Sicily</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a>) and south-eastern <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> had been colonized by the Greeks during the 8th century. In 4th-century Sicily the leading Greek city and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegemon" title="Hegemon">hegemon</a> was <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>. During the Hellenistic period the leading figure in Sicily was <a href="/wiki/Agathocles_of_Syracuse" title="Agathocles of Syracuse">Agathocles of Syracuse</a> (361&#8211;289 BC) who seized the city with an army of mercenaries in 317 BC. Agathocles extended his power throughout most of the Greek cities in Sicily, fought a long war with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthaginians" title="Carthaginians">Carthaginians</a>, at one point invading <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> in 310 and defeating a Carthaginian army there. This was the first time a European force had invaded the region. After this war he controlled most of south-east Sicily and had himself proclaimed king, in imitation of the Hellenistic monarchs of the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Agathocles then invaded Italy (c. 300 BC) in defense of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Tarentum</a> against the Bruttians and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, but was unsuccessful.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_(inscription_gallo-grecque).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="516" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_%28inscription_gallo-grecque%29.png/250px-D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_%28inscription_gallo-grecque%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_%28inscription_gallo-grecque%29.png/375px-D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_%28inscription_gallo-grecque%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_%28inscription_gallo-grecque%29.png/500px-D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_%28inscription_gallo-grecque%29.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D%C3%A9dicace_de_Segomaros_(inscription_gallo-grecque).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gallo-Greek inscription: "Segomaros, son of Uill&#363;, citizen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delamarre_2008_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delamarre_2008-40">[40]</a></sup> (<i>toutious</i>) of <a href="/wiki/N%C3%AEmes" title="N&#238;mes">Namausos</a>, dedicated this <a href="/wiki/Nemeton" title="Nemeton">sanctuary</a> to Belesama"</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2051" data-file-width="4282" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg/250px-Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg/375px-Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg/500px-Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Massalia_large_coin_5th_1st_century_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A silver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drachma" title="Drachma">drachma</a> from Massalia (modern <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, France), dated 375&#8211;200 BC, with the head of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obverse" title="Obverse">obverse</a> and a lion on the reverse</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_pre-Roman_Gaul" title="Greeks in pre-Roman Gaul">Greeks in pre-Roman Gaul</a> were mostly limited to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_France" title="History of France">France</a>. The first Greek colony in the region was <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massalia</a>, which became one of the largest trading ports of Mediterranean by the 4th century BC with 6,000 inhabitants. Massalia was also the local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegemon" title="Hegemon">hegemon</a>, controlling various coastal Greek cities like <a href="/wiki/Nice" title="Nice">Nice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agde" title="Agde">Agde</a>. The coins minted in Massalia have been found in all parts of Ligurian-Celtic Gaul. <a href="/wiki/Celtic_coinage" title="Celtic coinage">Celtic coinage</a> was influenced by Greek designs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and Greek letters can be found on various Celtic coins, especially those of <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">Southern France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beale_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beale-42">[42]</a></sup> Traders from Massalia ventured inland deep into France on the Rivers <a href="/wiki/Durance" title="Durance">Durance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rh&#244;ne">Rh&#244;ne</a>, and established overland trade routes deep into <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, and to <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burgundy" title="Burgundy">Burgundy</a>. The Hellenistic period saw the Greek alphabet spread into southern Gaul from Massalia (3rd and 2nd centuries BC) and according to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, Massalia was also a center of education, where <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> went to learn Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> A staunch ally of Rome, Massalia retained its independence until it sided with <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> in 49 BC and was then <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Massilia" title="Siege of Massilia">taken by Caesar\'s forces</a>.\n</p><p>The city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emporion" title="Emporion">Emporion</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Emp%C3%BAries" title="Emp&#250;ries">Emp&#250;ries</a>), originally founded by <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic-period</a> settlers from <a href="/wiki/Phocaea" title="Phocaea">Phocaea</a> and Massalia in the 6th century BC near the village of <a href="/wiki/Sant_Mart%C3%AD_d%27Emp%C3%BAries" title="Sant Mart&#237; d\'Emp&#250;ries">Sant Mart&#237; d\'Emp&#250;ries</a> (located on an offshore island that forms part of <a href="/wiki/L%27Escala" title="L\'Escala">L\'Escala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Spain" title="History of Spain">Spain</a>),<sup
