class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> was reestablished in the 5th century BC with a new city (<i>neapolis</i>) on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian mainland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Emporion contained a mixed population of Greek colonists and Iberian natives, and although <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> assert that they lived in different <a href="/wiki/Quarter_(urban_subdivision)" title="Quarter (urban subdivision)">quarters</a>, these two groups were eventually integrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The city became a dominant trading hub and center of Hellenistic civilization in Iberia, eventually siding with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthaginian_Empire" title="Carthaginian Empire">Carthaginian Empire</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a> (218&#8211;201 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> However, Emporion lost its political independence around 195 BC with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Citerior" title="Hispania Citerior">Hispania Citerior</a> and by the 1st century BC had become fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanized" title="Romanized">Romanized</a> in culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_Near_East">Hellenistic Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hellenistic Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Hellenistic states of Asia and Egypt were run by an occupying imperial elite of Greco-Macedonian administrators and governors propped up by a standing army of mercenaries and a small core of Greco-Macedonian settlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Promotion of immigration from Greece was important in the establishment of this system. Hellenistic monarchs ran their kingdoms as royal estates and most of the heavy tax revenues went into the military and paramilitary forces which preserved their rule from any kind of revolution. Macedonian and Hellenistic monarchs were expected to lead their armies on the field, along with a group of privileged aristocratic companions or friends (<i>hetairoi, philoi</i>) which dined and drank with the king and acted as his advisory council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The monarch was also expected to serve as a charitable patron of the people; this public philanthropy could mean building projects and handing out gifts but also promotion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_culture" title="Greek culture">Greek culture</a> and religion.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ptolemaic_Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ptolemaic Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4050" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg/200px-Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg/300px-Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg/400px-Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_I_Soter_Louvre_Ma849.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy I Soter</a> wearing a <a href="/wiki/Diadem" title="Diadem">diadem</a>, a symbol of Hellenistic kingship, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatophylax" title="Somatophylax">somatophylax</a>, one of the seven <a href="/wiki/Bodyguard" title="Bodyguard">bodyguards</a> who served as <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>\'s generals and deputies, was appointed <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> after Alexander\'s death in 323 BC. In 305 BC, he declared himself King Ptolemy I, later known as "Soter" (saviour) for his role in helping the Rhodians during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Rhodes_(305_BC)" title="Siege of Rhodes (305 BC)">siege of Rhodes</a>. Ptolemy built new cities such as <a href="/wiki/Ptolemais_Hermiou" title="Ptolemais Hermiou">Ptolemais Hermiou</a> in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">upper Egypt</a> and settled his veterans throughout the country, especially in the region of the <a href="/wiki/Faiyum" title="Faiyum">Faiyum</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, a major center of Greek culture and trade, became his capital city. As Egypt\'s first port city, it was the main grain exporter in the Mediterranean.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> begrudgingly accepted the Ptolemies as the successors to the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaohs</a> of independent Egypt, though the kingdom went through several native revolts. The Ptolemies took on the traditions of the Egyptian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharaohs" title="Pharaohs">Pharaohs</a>, such as marrying their siblings (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II" title="Ptolemy II">Ptolemy II</a> was the first to adopt this custom), having themselves portrayed on public monuments in Egyptian style and dress, and participating in Egyptian religious life. The Ptolemaic ruler cult portrayed the Ptolemies as gods, and temples to the Ptolemies were erected throughout the kingdom. Ptolemy I even created a new god, <a href="/wiki/Serapis" title="Serapis">Serapis</a>, who was combination of two Egyptian gods: Apis and Osiris, with attributes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_gods" title="Greek gods">Greek gods</a>. Ptolemaic administration was, like the Ancient Egyptian bureaucracy, highly centralized and focused on squeezing as much revenue out of the population as possible though tariffs, excise duties, fines, taxes and so forth. A whole class of petty officials, tax farmers, clerks and overseers made this possible. The Egyptian countryside was directly administered by this royal bureaucracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> External possessions such as Cyprus and Cyrene were run by <i>strategoi</i>, military commanders appointed by the crown.  \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_(3rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE)_-_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1499" data-file-width="1155" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_%283rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE%29_-_2009.jpg/220px-Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_%283rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE%29_-_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_%283rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE%29_-_2009.jpg/330px-Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_%283rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE%29_-_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_%283rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE%29_-_2009.jpg/440px-Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_%283rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE%29_-_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ring_with_engraved_portrait_of_Ptolemy_VI_Philometor_(3rd%E2%80%932nd_century_BCE)_-_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ring of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_VI_Philometor" title="Ptolemy VI Philometor">Ptolemy VI Philometor</a> as Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II" title="Ptolemy II">Ptolemy II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theocritus" title="Theocritus">Theocritus</a>, and a host of other poets including the <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_Pleiad" title="Alexandrian Pleiad">Alexandrian Pleiad</a> made the city a center of Hellenistic literature. Ptolemy himself was eager to patronise the library, scientific research and individual scholars who lived on the grounds of the library. He and his successors also fought a series of wars with the Seleucids, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_wars" title="Syrian wars">Syrian wars</a>, over the region of <a href="/wiki/Coele-Syria" title="Coele-Syria">Coele-Syria</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_IV" title="Ptolemy IV">Ptolemy IV</a> won the great <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Raphia" title="Battle of Raphia">battle of Raphia</a> (217 BC) against the Seleucids, using native Egyptians trained as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalangites" title="Phalangites">phalangites</a>. However these Egyptian soldiers revolted, eventually setting up a native breakaway Egyptian state in the <a href="/wiki/Thebaid" title="Thebaid">Thebaid</a> between 205&#8211;186/5 BC, severely weakening the Ptolemaic state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ptolemy\'s family ruled Egypt until the <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Roman</a> conquest of 30 BC. All the male rulers of the dynasty took the name Ptolemy. Ptolemaic queens, some of whom were the sisters of their husbands, were usually called Cleopatra, Arsinoe, or Berenice. The most famous member of the line was the last queen, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a>, known for her role in the Roman political battles between <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>, and later between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_Caesar" title="Augustus Caesar">Octavian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a>. Her suicide at the conquest by Rome marked the end of Ptolemaic rule in Egypt though Hellenistic culture continued to thrive in Egypt throughout the Roman and Byzantine periods until the Muslim conquest.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seleucid_Empire">Seleucid Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Seleucid Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG/200px-Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG/300px-Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG/400px-Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seleuco_I_Nicatore.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a></div>\n<p>Following division of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a>\'s empire, <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a> received <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>. From there, he created a new empire which expanded to include much of Alexander\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_east" title="Near east">near eastern</a> territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones,_Kenneth_Raymond_2006_174_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones,_Kenneth_Raymond_2006_174-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Society_for_the_Promotion_of_Hellenic_Studies_(London,_England)_1993_211_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Society_for_the_Promotion_of_Hellenic_Studies_(London,_England)_1993_211-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baskin,_Judith_R.;_Seeskin,_Kenneth_2010_37_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baskin,_Judith_R.;_Seeskin,_Kenneth_2010_37-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glubb,_Sir_John_Bagot_1967_34_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glubb,_Sir_John_Bagot_1967_34-57">[57]</a></sup> At the height of its power, it included central <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, today\'s <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>. It included a diverse population estimated at fifty to sixty million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\nUnder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_I" title="Antiochus I">Antiochus I</a> (c. 324/3 &#8211; 261 BC), however, the unwieldy empire was already beginning to shed territories. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pergamum" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a> broke away under <a href="/wiki/Eumenes_I" title="Eumenes I">Eumenes I</a> who defeated a Seleucid army sent against him. The kingdoms of Cappadocia, Bithynia and Pontus were all practically independent by this time as well. Like the Ptolemies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_I" title="Antiochus I">Antiochus I</a> established a dynastic religious cult, deifying his father <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucus_I" title="Seleucus I">Seleucus I</a>. Seleucus, officially said to be descended from Apollo, had his own priests and monthly sacrifices. The erosion of the empire continued under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucus_II" title="Seleucus II">Seleucus II</a>, who was forced to fight a civil war (239&#8211;236) against his brother <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_Hierax" title="Antiochus Hierax">Antiochus Hierax</a> and was unable to keep <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> from breaking away. Hierax carved off most of Seleucid Anatolia for himself, but was defeated, along with his Galatian allies, by <a href="/wiki/Attalus_I" title="Attalus I">Attalus I</a> of Pergamon who now also claimed kingship.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="1205" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg/300px-Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg/450px-Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg/600px-Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rome-Seleucia-Parthia_200bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hellenistic world c. 200 BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>The vast Seleucid Empire was, like Egypt, mostly dominated by a Greco-Macedonian political elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glubb,_Sir_John_Bagot_1967_34_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glubb,_Sir_John_Bagot_1967_34-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steven_C._Hause,_William_S._Maltby_2004_76_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steven_C._Hause,_William_S._Maltby_2004_76-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Victor,_Royce_M._2010_55_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Victor,_Royce_M._2010_55-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brit_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brit-61">[61]</a></sup> The Greek population of the cities who formed the dominant elite were reinforced by emigration from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glubb,_Sir_John_Bagot_1967_34_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Glubb,_Sir_John_Bagot_1967_34-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steven_C._Hause,_William_S._Maltby_2004_76_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Steven_C._Hause,_William_S._Maltby_2004_76-59">[59]</a></sup> These cities included newly founded colonies such as <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, the other cities of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_tetrapolis" title="Syrian tetrapolis">Syrian tetrapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> (north of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Dura-Europos" title="Dura-Europos">Dura-Europos</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>. These cities retained traditional Greek city state institutions such as assemblies, councils and elected magistrates, but this was a facade for they were always controlled by the royal Seleucid officials. Apart from these cities, there were also a large number of Seleucid garrisons (<i>choria</i>), military colonies (<i>katoikiai</i>) and Greek villages (<i>komai</i>) which the Seleucids planted throughout the empire to cement their rule. This \'Greco-Macedonian\' population (which also included the sons of settlers who had married local women) could make up a phalanx of 35,000 men (out of a total Seleucid army of 80,000) during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochos_III" title="Antiochos III">Antiochos III</a>. The rest of the army was made up of native troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_III" title="Antiochus III">Antiochus III</a> ("the Great") conducted several vigorous campaigns to retake all the lost provinces of the empire since the death of Seleucus I. After being defeated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_IV" title="Ptolemy IV">Ptolemy IV</a>\'s forces <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Raphia" title="Battle of Raphia">at Raphia</a> (217), Antiochus III led a long campaign to the east to subdue the far eastern breakaway provinces (212-205) including <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">Ariana</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gedrosia" title="Gedrosia">Gedrosia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drangiana" title="Drangiana">Drangiana</a>. He was successful, bringing back most of these provinces into at least nominal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vassalage" title="Vassalage">vassalage</a> and receiving tribute from their rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> After the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_IV" title="Ptolemy IV">Ptolemy IV</a> (204), Antiochus took advantage of the weakness of Egypt to conquer Coele-Syria in the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Wars" title="Syrian Wars">fifth Syrian war</a> (202&#8211;195).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> He then began expanding his influence into Pergamene territory in Asia and crossed into Europe, fortifying <a href="/wiki/Lysimachia_(Thrace)" title="Lysimachia (Thrace)">Lysimachia</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellespont" title="Hellespont">Hellespont</a>, but his expansion into <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and Greece was abruptly halted after a decisive defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magnesia" title="Battle of Magnesia">Battle of Magnesia</a> (190 BC). In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Apamea" title="Treaty of Apamea">Treaty of Apamea</a> which ended the war, Antiochus lost all of his territories in Anatolia west of the Taurus and was forced to pay a large indemnity of 15,000 talents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of the eastern part of the empire was then conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates I of Parthia">Mithridates I of Parthia</a> in the mid-2nd century BC, yet the Seleucid kings continued to rule a <a href="/wiki/Rump_state" title="Rump state">rump state</a> from <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> until the invasion by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Armenian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_the_Great" title="Tigranes the Great">Tigranes the Great</a> and their ultimate overthrow by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> general <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attalid_Pergamum">Attalid Pergamum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Attalid Pergamum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pergamum" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a></div>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus,_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_(5336871341).jpg"><img alt="Athena and Nike fight Alkyoneus, Gaia rises up from the ground (5336871341).jpg" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus%2C_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_%285336871341%29.jpg/210px-Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus%2C_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_%285336871341%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus%2C_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_%285336871341%29.jpg/315px-Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus%2C_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_%285336871341%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus%2C_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_%285336871341%29.jpg/420px-Athena_and_Nike_fight_Alkyoneus%2C_Gaia_rises_up_from_the_ground_%285336871341%29.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="http://smarthistory.khanacademy.org/the-pergamon-altar1.html" rel="nofollow">The Pergamon Altar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smarthistory" title="Smarthistory">Smarthistory</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smarth_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-smarth-66">[66]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a>, one of his officers, <a href="/wiki/Philetaerus" title="Philetaerus">Philetaerus</a>, took control of the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pergamum" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a> in 282 BC along with Lysimachus\' war chest of 9,000 talents and declared himself loyal to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucus_I" title="Seleucus I">Seleucus I</a> while remaining <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> independent. His descendant, <a href="/wiki/Attalus_I" title="Attalus I">Attalus I</a>, defeated the invading <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatians</a> and proclaimed himself an independent king. <a href="/wiki/Attalus_I" title="Attalus I">Attalus I</a> (241&#8211;197 BC), was a staunch ally of Rome against <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V of Macedon</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">first</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Macedonian_War" title="Second Macedonian War">second</a> <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Wars" title="Macedonian Wars">Macedonian Wars</a>. For his support against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid_dynasty" title="Seleucid dynasty">Seleucids</a> in 190 BC, <a href="/wiki/Eumenes_II" title="Eumenes II">Eumenes II</a> was rewarded with all the former Seleucid domains in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. Eumenes II turned Pergamon into a centre of culture and science by establishing the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Pergamum" title="Library of Pergamum">library of Pergamum</a> which was said to be second only to the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">library of Alexandria</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> with 200,000 volumes according to <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>. It included a reading room and a collection of paintings. Eumenes II also constructed the <a href="/wiki/Pergamon_Altar" title="Pergamon Altar">Pergamum Altar</a> with friezes depicting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigantomachy" title="Gigantomachy">Gigantomachy</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis" title="Acropolis">acropolis</a> of the city. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pergamum" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a> was also a center of <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a> (<i>charta pergamena</i>) production. The Attalids ruled Pergamon until <a href="/wiki/Attalus_III" title="Attalus III">Attalus III</a> bequeathed the kingdom to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> in 133&#160;BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> to avoid a likely succession crisis.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galatia">Galatia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Galatia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_%281%29.jpg/310px-0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_%281%29.jpg/465px-0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_%281%29.jpg/620px-0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0_Galata_Morente_-_Musei_Capitolini_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dying_Gaul" title="The Dying Gaul">The Dying Gaul</a> is a Roman marble copy of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> work of the late 3rd century BC. <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a>, Rome.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Celts who settled in <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatia</a> came through <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> under the leadership of Leotarios and <a href="/wiki/Leonnorius" title="Leonnorius">Leonnorios</a> c. 270 BC. They were defeated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucus_I" title="Seleucus I">Seleucus I</a> in the \'battle of the Elephants\', but were still able to establish a Celtic territory in central <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. The Galatians were well respected as warriors and were widely used as mercenaries in the armies of the successor states. They continued to attack neighboring kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a>, plundering and extracting tribute. This came to an end when they sided with the renegade Seleucid prince <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_Hierax" title="Antiochus Hierax">Antiochus Hierax</a> who tried to defeat <a href="/wiki/Attalus_I" title="Attalus I">Attalus</a>, the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a> (241&#8211;197 BC). Attalus severely defeated the Gauls, forcing them to confine themselves to Galatia. The theme of the <i><a href="/wiki/Dying_Gaul" title="Dying Gaul">Dying Gaul</a></i> (a famous statue displayed in <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a>) remained a favorite in Hellenistic art for a generation signifying the victory of the Greeks over a noble enemy. In the early 2nd century BC, the Galatians became allies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_III" title="Antiochus III">Antiochus the Great</a>, the last Seleucid king trying to regain suzerainty over Asia Minor. In 189 BC, Rome sent <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Manlius_Vulso" title="Gnaeus Manlius Vulso">Gnaeus Manlius Vulso</a> on an expedition against the Galatians. Galatia was henceforth dominated by Rome through regional rulers from 189 BC onward.\n</p><p>After their defeats by Pergamon and Rome the Galatians slowly became hellenized and they were called "Gallo-Graeci" by the historian <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> as well as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#7961;&#955;&#955;&#951;&#957;&#959;&#947;&#945;&#955;&#940;&#964;&#945;&#953;</span> (<i>Hell&#275;nogal&#225;tai</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> in his <i>Bibliotheca historica</i> v.32.5, who wrote that they were "called Helleno-Galatians because of their connection with the Greeks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bithynia">Bithynia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bithynia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a></div>\n<p>The Bithynians were a Thracian people living in northwest Anatolia. After Alexander\'s conquests the region of Bithynia came under the rule of the native king Bas, who defeated Calas, a general of Alexander the Great, and maintained the independence of Bithynia. His son, <a href="/wiki/Zipoetes_I_of_Bithynia" title="Zipoetes I of Bithynia">Zipoetes I of Bithynia</a> maintained this autonomy against <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucus_I" title="Seleucus I">Seleucus I</a>, and assumed the title of king (<i>basileus</i>) in 297 BC. His son and successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicomedes_I" title="Nicomedes I">Nicomedes I</a>, founded <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a>, which soon rose to great prosperity, and during his long reign (c. 278 &#8211; c. 255 BC), as well as those of his successors, the kingdom of Bithynia held a considerable place among the minor monarchies of Anatolia. Nicomedes also invited the Celtic Galatians into Anatolia as mercenaries, and they later turned on his son Prusias I, who defeated them in battle. Their last king, Nicomedes IV, was unable to maintain himself against Mithridates VI of Pontus, and, after being restored to his throne by the Roman Senate, he bequeathed his kingdom by will to the Roman republic (74 BC).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cappadocia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a></div>\n<p>Cappadocia, a mountainous region situated between Pontus and the Taurus mountains, was ruled by a Persian dynasty. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ariarathes_I" title="Ariarathes I">Ariarathes I</a> (332&#8211;322 BC) was the satrap of Cappadocia under the Persians and after the conquests of Alexander he retained his post. After Alexander\'s death he was defeated by Eumenes and crucified in 322 BC, but his son, Ariarathes II managed to regain the throne and maintain his autonomy against the warring Diadochi.\n</p><p>In 255 BC, Ariarathes III took the title of king and married Stratonice, a daughter of Antiochus II, remaining an ally of the Seleucid kingdom. Under Ariarathes IV, Cappadocia came into relations with Rome, first as a foe espousing the cause of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_the_Great" title="Antiochus the Great">Antiochus the Great</a>, then as an ally against Perseus of Macedon and finally in a war against the Seleucids. Ariarathes V also waged war with Rome against Aristonicus, a claimant to the throne of Pergamon, and their forces were annihilated in 130 BC. This defeat allowed Pontus to invade and conquer the kingdom.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Pontus">Kingdom of Pontus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Pontus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">Kingdom of Pontus</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg/200px-Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg/300px-Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg/400px-Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mithridates_VI_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI" title="Mithridates VI">Mithridates VI</a> sporting a lion pelt headdress, a symbol of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herakles" title="Herakles">Herakles</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">Kingdom of Pontus</a> was a Hellenistic kingdom on the southern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. It was founded by <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Pontus" title="Mithridates I of Pontus">Mithridates</a> I in 291 BC and lasted until its conquest by the Roman Republic in 63 BC. Despite being ruled by a dynasty which was a descendant of the Persian <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> it became hellenized due to the influence of the Greek cities on the Black Sea and its neighboring kingdoms. Pontic culture was a mix of Greek and Iranian elements; the most hellenized parts of the kingdom were on the coast, populated by Greek colonies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trapezus,_Arcadia" title="Trapezus, Arcadia">Trapezus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sinop,_Turkey" title="Sinop, Turkey">Sinope</a>, the latter of which became the capital of the kingdom. Epigraphic evidence also shows extensive Hellenistic influence in the interior. During the reign of Mithridates II, Pontus was allied with the Seleucids through dynastic marriages. By the time of Mithridates VI Eupator, Greek was the official language of the kingdom, though Anatolian languages continued to be spoken.\n</p><p>The kingdom grew to its largest extent under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI" title="Mithridates VI">Mithridates VI</a>, who conquered <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paphlagonia" title="Paphlagonia">Paphlagonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>, Lesser Armenia, the <a href="/wiki/Bosporan_Kingdom" title="Bosporan Kingdom">Bosporan Kingdom</a>, the Greek colonies of the Tauric <a href="/wiki/Chersonesus" title="Chersonesus">Chersonesos</a> and, for a brief time, the Roman province of Asia. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI" title="Mithridates VI">Mithridates VI</a>, himself of mixed Persian and Greek ancestry, presented himself as the protector of the Greeks against the \'barbarians\' of Rome styling himself as "King Mithridates Eupator Dionysus"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Foreign_Policy_of_Mithridates_VI_Eupator,_King_of_Pontus_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Foreign_Policy_of_Mithridates_VI_Eupator,_King_of_Pontus-71">[71]</a></sup> and as the "great liberator". Mithridates also depicted himself with the <i>anastole</i> hairstyle of Alexander and used the symbolism of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herakles" title="Herakles">Herakles</a>, from whom the Macedonian kings claimed descent. After a long struggle with Rome in the Mithridatic wars, Pontus was defeated; part of it was incorporated into the Roman Republic as the province of Bithynia, while Pontus\' eastern half survived as a client kingdom.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenia">Armenia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Armenia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="673" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif/230px-Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif/345px-Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif/460px-Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maps_of_the_Armenian_Empire_of_Tigranes.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tigranes the Great\'s Armenian Empire</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orontid_Dynasty" title="Orontid Dynasty">Orontid Armenia</a> formally passed to the empire of Alexander the Great following his conquest of Persia. Alexander appointed an Orontid named Mithranes to govern Armenia. Armenia later became a vassal state of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, but it maintained a considerable degree of autonomy, retaining its native rulers. Towards the end 212 BC the country was divided into two kingdoms, Greater Armenia and Armenia <a href="/wiki/Sophene" title="Sophene">Sophene</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commagene" title="Commagene">Commagene</a> or Armenia Minor. The kingdoms became so independent from Seleucid control that <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_III_the_Great" title="Antiochus III the Great">Antiochus III the Great</a> waged war on them during his reign and replaced their rulers.\n</p><p>After the Seleucid defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magnesia" title="Battle of Magnesia">Battle of Magnesia</a> in 190 BC, the kings of <a href="/wiki/Sophene" title="Sophene">Sophene</a> and Greater Armenia revolted and declared their independence, with <a href="/wiki/Artaxias_I" title="Artaxias I">Artaxias</a> becoming the first king of the <a href="/wiki/Artaxiad_dynasty" title="Artaxiad dynasty">Artaxiad dynasty</a> of Armenia in 188. During the reign of the Artaxiads, Armenia went through a period of <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">hellenization</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numismatic" title="Numismatic">Numismatic</a> evidence shows Greek artistic styles and the use of the Greek language. Some coins describe the Armenian kings as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philhellenes" title="Philhellenes">Philhellenes</a>". During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_the_Great" title="Tigranes the Great">Tigranes the Great</a> (95&#8211;55 BC), the kingdom of Armenia reached its greatest extent, containing many Greek cities, including the entire <a href="/wiki/Syrian_tetrapolis" title="Syrian tetrapolis">Syrian tetrapolis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_of_Pontus" title="Cleopatra of Pontus">Cleopatra</a>, the wife of <a href="/wiki/Tigranes_the_Great" title="Tigranes the Great">Tigranes the Great</a>, invited Greeks such as the <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetor</a> Amphicrates and the historian <a href="/wiki/Metrodorus_of_Scepsis" title="Metrodorus of Scepsis">Metrodorus of Scepsis</a> to the Armenian court, and&#8212;according to Plutarch&#8212;when the Roman general Lucullus seized the Armenian capital, Tigranocerta, he found a troupe of Greek actors who had arrived to perform plays for Tigranes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Tigranes\' successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artavasdes_II" title="Artavasdes II">Artavasdes II</a> even composed Greek tragedies himself.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parthia">Parthia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Parthia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orodesi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="226" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Orodesi.jpg/220px-Orodesi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Orodesi.jpg/330px-Orodesi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Orodesi.jpg/440px-Orodesi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orodesi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Phraates_IV" title="Phraates IV">Phraates IV</a> with Hellenistic titles such as Euergetes, Epiphanes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philhellene" title="Philhellene">Philhellene</a> (admirer of the Greeks)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> was a north-eastern Iranian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satrapy" title="Satrapy">satrapy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> which later passed on to Alexander\'s empire. Under the Seleucids, Parthia was governed by various Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satraps" title="Satraps">satraps</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Nicanor_(satrap)" title="Nicanor (satrap)">Nicanor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_(satrap)" title="Philip (satrap)">Philip</a>. In 247 BC, following the death of <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_II_Theos" title="Antiochus II Theos">Antiochus II Theos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andragoras_(Seleucid_satrap)" title="Andragoras (Seleucid satrap)">Andragoras</a>, the Seleucid governor of Parthia, proclaimed his independence and began minting coins showing himself wearing a royal diadem and claiming kingship. He ruled until 238 BC when <a href="/wiki/Arsaces_I_of_Parthia" title="Arsaces I of Parthia">Arsaces</a>, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Parni" title="Parni">Parni</a> tribe conquered Parthia, killing Andragoras and inaugurating the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Arsacid Dynasty</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_III" title="Antiochus III">Antiochus III</a> recaptured Arsacid controlled territory in 209&#160;BC from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arsaces_II" title="Arsaces II">Arsaces II</a>. Arsaces II sued for peace and became a vassal of the Seleucids. It was not until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Phraates_I" title="Phraates I">Phraates I</a> (168&#8211;165 BC), that the Arsacids would again begin to assert their independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates I of Parthia">Mithridates I of Parthia</a>, Arsacid control expanded to include <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> (in 167 BC), <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> (in 144 BC), <a href="/wiki/Media_(region)" title="Media (region)">Media</a> (in 141 BC), <a href="/wiki/Persis" title="Persis">Persia</a> (in 139 BC), and large parts of <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syria</a> (in the 110s BC). The <a href="/wiki/Seleucid%E2%80%93Parthian_wars" title="Seleucid&#8211;Parthian wars">Seleucid&#8211;Parthian wars</a> continued as the Seleucids invaded Mesopotamia under <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_VII_Sidetes" title="Antiochus VII Sidetes">Antiochus VII Sidetes</a> (r. 138&#8211;129 BC), but he was eventually killed by a Parthian counterattack. After the fall of the Seleucid dynasty, the Parthians fought frequently against neighbouring Rome in the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_Wars" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian Wars">Roman&#8211;Parthian Wars</a> (66 BC &#8211; 217 AD). Abundant traces of Hellenism continued under the Parthian empire. The Parthians used Greek as well as their own Parthian language (though lesser than Greek) as languages of administration and also used Greek drachmas as coinage. They enjoyed Greek theater, and Greek art influenced <a href="/wiki/Parthian_art" title="Parthian art">Parthian art</a>. The Parthians continued worshipping Greek gods <a href="/wiki/Syncretism#Religious_syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretized</a> together with Iranian deities. Their rulers established ruler cults in the manner of Hellenistic kings and often used Hellenistic royal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epithets" title="Epithets">epithets</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nabatean_Kingdom">Nabatean Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nabatean Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabatean_Kingdom" title="Nabatean Kingdom">Nabatean Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3359" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG/170px-Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG/255px-Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG/340px-Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Petra_Jordan_BW_21.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Al-Khazneh" title="Al-Khazneh">Al-Khazneh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> shows the Hellenistic influences on the Nabatean capital city</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabatean_Kingdom" title="Nabatean Kingdom">Nabatean Kingdom</a> was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> state located between the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>. Its capital was the city of <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a>, an important trading city on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incense_route" title="Incense route">incense route</a>. The Nabateans resisted the attacks of <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_I_Monophthalmus" title="Antigonus I Monophthalmus">Antigonus</a> and were allies of the Hasmoneans in their struggle against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucids" title="Seleucids">Seleucids</a>, but later fought against <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">Herod the Great</a>. The hellenization of the Nabateans occurred relatively late in comparison to the surrounding regions. Nabatean <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> does not show any Greek influence until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Aretas_III" title="Aretas III">Aretas III</a> Philhellene in the 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Aretas captured <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> and built the Petra pool complex and gardens in the Hellenistic style. Though the Nabateans originally worshipped their traditional gods in symbolic form such as stone blocks or pillars, during the Hellenistic period they began to identify their gods with Greek gods and depict them in figurative forms influenced by Greek sculpture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Nabatean art shows Greek influences and paintings have been found depicting <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysian</a> scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> They also slowly adopted Greek as a language of commerce along with Aramaic and Arabic.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judea">Judea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Judea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coele-Syria" title="Coele-Syria">Coele-Syria</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hasmonean_dynasty" title="Hasmonean dynasty">Hasmonean dynasty</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="2472" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG/170px-Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG/255px-Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG/340px-Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jerusalem_Modell_BW_3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Holyland_Model_of_Jerusalem" title="Holyland Model of Jerusalem">Model</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herod%27s_Temple" title="Herod\'s Temple">Herod\'s Temple</a> (renovation of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Israel_Museum" title="Israel Museum">Israel Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During the Hellenistic period, <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> became a frontier region between the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> and therefore was often the frontline of the Syrian wars, changing hands several times during these conflicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Under the Hellenistic kingdoms, Judea was ruled by the hereditary office of the <a href="/wiki/High_Priest_of_Israel" title="High Priest of Israel">High Priest of Israel</a> as a Hellenistic vassal. This period also saw the rise of a <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a>, which first developed in the Jewish diaspora of Alexandria and Antioch, and then spread to Judea. The major literary product of this cultural syncretism is the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> translation of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> from <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">Biblical Aramaic</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_Greek" title="Koin&#233; Greek">Koin&#233; Greek</a>. The reason for the production of this translation seems to be that many of the Alexandrian Jews had lost the ability to speak Hebrew and Aramaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 301 and 219 BC the Ptolemies ruled Judea in relative peace, and Jews often found themselves working in the Ptolemaic administration and army, which led to the rise of a Hellenized Jewish elite class (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Tobiads" title="Tobiads">Tobiads</a>). The wars of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_III" title="Antiochus III">Antiochus III</a> brought the region into the Seleucid empire; Jerusalem fell to his control in 198 and the Temple was repaired and provided with money and tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_IV_Epiphanes" title="Antiochus IV Epiphanes">Antiochus IV Epiphanes</a> sacked Jerusalem and looted the Temple in 169 BC after disturbances in Judea during his abortive invasion of Egypt. Antiochus then banned key <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish religious rites and traditions</a> in Judea. He may have been attempting to Hellenize the region and unify his empire and the Jewish resistance to this eventually led to an escalation of violence. Whatever the case, tensions between pro and anti-Seleucid Jewish factions led to the 174&#8211;135 BC <a href="/wiki/Maccabean_Revolt" title="Maccabean Revolt">Maccabean Revolt</a> of <a href="/wiki/Judas_Maccabeus" title="Judas Maccabeus">Judas Maccabeus</a> (whose victory is celebrated in the Jewish festival of <a href="/wiki/Hanukkah" title="Hanukkah">Hanukkah</a>).\n</p><p>Modern interpretations see this period as a civil war between Hellenized and orthodox forms of Judaism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simpletoremember_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-simpletoremember-81">[81]</a></sup> Out of this revolt was formed an independent Jewish kingdom known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasmonaean_Dynasty" title="Hasmonaean Dynasty">Hasmonaean Dynasty</a>, which lasted from 165 BC to 63 BC. The Hasmonean Dynasty eventually disintegrated in <a href="/wiki/Hyrcanus_II#Deposition" title="Hyrcanus II">a civil war</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Hyrcanus_II#Roman_intervention" title="Hyrcanus II">coincided with civil wars in Rome</a>. The last Hasmonean ruler, <a href="/wiki/Antigonus_II_Mattathias" title="Antigonus II Mattathias">Antigonus II Mattathias</a>, was captured by Herod and executed in 37 BC. In spite of originally being a revolt against Greek overlordship, the Hasmonean kingdom and also the <a href="/wiki/Herodian_kingdom" title="Herodian kingdom">Herodian kingdom</a> which followed gradually became more and more hellenized. From 37 BC to 4 BC, <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">Herod the Great</a> ruled as a Jewish-Roman client king appointed by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a>. He considerably enlarged the Temple (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herod%27s_Temple" title="Herod\'s Temple">Herod\'s Temple</a>), making it one of the largest religious structures in the world. The style of the enlarged temple and other <a href="/wiki/Herodian_architecture" title="Herodian architecture">Herodian architecture</a> shows significant Hellenistic architectural influence. His son, <a href="/wiki/Herod_Archelaus" title="Herod Archelaus">Herod Archelaus</a>, ruled from 4 BC to 6 AD when he was deposed for the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Judea" title="Roman Judea">Roman Judea</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Greco-Bactrians">Greco-Bactrians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Greco-Bactrians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a></div><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_influence_on_Indian_art" title="Hellenistic influence on Indian art">Hellenistic influence on Indian art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1311" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg/220px-Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg/330px-Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg/440px-Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greco-BactrianKingdomMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Greco-Bactrian kingdom at its maximum extent (c. 180 BC).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DemetriusIMet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1153" data-file-width="1162" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/DemetriusIMet.jpg/180px-DemetriusIMet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/DemetriusIMet.jpg/270px-DemetriusIMet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/DemetriusIMet.jpg/360px-DemetriusIMet.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DemetriusIMet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver coin depicting <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_of_Bactria" title="Demetrius I of Bactria">Demetrius I of Bactria</a> (reigned c. 200&#8211;180 BC), wearing an elephant scalp, symbol of his conquests of areas in the northwest of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> are today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Greek kingdom of Bactria began as a breakaway satrapy of the Seleucid empire, which, because of the size of the empire, had significant freedom from central control. Between 255-246 BC, the governor of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Margiana</a> (most of present-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>), one <a href="/wiki/Diodotus_I" title="Diodotus I">Diodotus</a>, took this process to its logical extreme and declared himself king. Diodotus II, son of Diodotus, was overthrown in about 230 BC by <a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_I" title="Euthydemus I">Euthydemus</a>, possibly the satrap of Sogdiana, who then started his own dynasty. In c. 210 BC, the Greco-Bactrian kingdom was invaded by a resurgent Seleucid empire under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_III" title="Antiochus III">Antiochus III</a>. While victorious in the field, it seems Antiochus came to realise that there were advantages in the status quo (perhaps sensing that Bactria could not be governed from Syria), and married one of his daughters to Euthydemus\'s son, thus legitimising the Greco-Bactrian dynasty. Soon afterwards the Greco-Bactrian kingdom seems to have expanded, possibly taking advantage of the defeat of the Parthian king <a href="/wiki/Arsaces_II_of_Parthia" title="Arsaces II of Parthia">Arsaces II</a> by Antiochus.\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, the Greco-Bactrians seem to have had contacts with China through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silk_road" title="Silk road">silk road</a> trade routes (Strabo, XI.XI.I). Indian sources also maintain religious contact between Buddhist monks and the Greeks, and some Greco-Bactrians did convert to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_I_of_Bactria" title="Demetrius I of Bactria">Demetrius</a>, son and successor of Euthydemus, invaded north-western India in 180 BC, after the destruction of the Mauryan Empire there; the Mauryans were probably allies of the Bactrians (and Seleucids). The exact justification for the invasion remains unclear, but by about 175 BC, the Greeks ruled over parts of north-western India. This period also marks the beginning of the obfuscation of Greco-Bactrian history. Demetrius possibly died about 180 BC; numismatic evidence suggests the existence of several other kings shortly thereafter. It is probable that at this point the Greco-Bactrian kingdom split into several semi-independent regions for some years, often warring amongst themselves. Heliocles was the last Greek to clearly rule Bactria, his power collapsing in the face of central Asian tribal invasions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scythian" title="Scythian">Scythian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>), by about 130 BC. However, Greek urban civilisation seems to have continued in Bactria after the fall of the kingdom, having a hellenising effect on the tribes which had displaced Greek rule. The <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> which followed continued to use Greek on their coinage and Greeks continued being influential in the empire.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indo-Greek_kingdoms">Indo-Greek kingdoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Indo-Greek kingdoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greeks" title="Indo-Greeks">Indo-Greeks</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_religions" title="Indo-Greek religions">Indo-Greek religions</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="880" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg/220px-Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg/330px-Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg/440px-Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indo-Greeks_100bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indo-Greek Kingdoms in 100 BC.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1068" data-file-width="983" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG/180px-Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG/270px-Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG/360px-Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> as protector of Buddha, <a href="/wiki/Vajrapani" title="Vajrapani">Vajrapani</a>, 2nd-century <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The separation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_kingdom" title="Indo-Greek kingdom">Indo-Greek kingdom</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a> resulted in an even more isolated position, and thus the details of the Indo-Greek kingdom are even more obscure than for Bactria. Many supposed kings in India are known only because of coins bearing their name. The numismatic evidence together with archaeological finds and the scant historical records suggest that the fusion of eastern and western cultures reached its peak in the Indo-Greek kingdom.\n</p><p>After Demetrius\' death, civil wars between Bactrian kings in India allowed <a href="/wiki/Apollodotus_I" title="Apollodotus I">Apollodotus I</a> (from c. 180/175 BC) to make himself independent as the first proper Indo-Greek king (who did not rule from Bactria). Large numbers of his coins have been found in India, and he seems to have reigned in <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> as well as western <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>. Apollodotus I was succeeded by or ruled alongside <a href="/wiki/Antimachus_II" title="Antimachus II">Antimachus II</a>, likely the son of the Bactrian king <a href="/wiki/Antimachus_I" title="Antimachus I">Antimachus I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In about 155 (or 165) BC he seems to have been succeeded by the most successful of the Indo-Greek kings, <a href="/wiki/Menander_I" title="Menander I">Menander I</a>. Menander converted to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and seems to have been a great patron of the religion; he is remembered in some Buddhist texts as \'Milinda\'. He also expanded the kingdom further east into Punjab, though these conquests were rather ephemeral.\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:330px;max-width:330px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:124px;max-width:124px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="536" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg/122px-Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg/183px-Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg/244px-Heliodorus_pillar_inscription.jpg 2x" width="122"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center>The <a href="/wiki/Heliodorus_pillar" title="Heliodorus pillar">Heliodorus pillar</a>, commissioned by Indo-Greek ambassador <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliodorus_(votive_erector)" title="Heliodorus (votive erector)">Heliodorus</a>, one of the earliest recorded Indo-Greek converts to <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></center></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MenandrosCoin.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="1079" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/MenandrosCoin.jpg/200px-MenandrosCoin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/MenandrosCoin.jpg/300px-MenandrosCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/MenandrosCoin.jpg/400px-MenandrosCoin.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center><a href="/wiki/Menander_I" title="Menander I">Menander I</a> converted to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, as described in the <a href="/wiki/Milinda_Panha" title="Milinda Panha">Milinda Panha</a>. After his conversion, he became noted for being a leading patron of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Buddhism" title="History of Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></center></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>After the death of Menander (c. 130 BC), the Kingdom appears to have fragmented, with several \'kings\' attested contemporaneously in different regions. This inevitably weakened the Greek position, and territory seems to have been lost progressively. Around 70 BC, the western regions of <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paropamisadae" title="Paropamisadae">Paropamisadae</a> were lost to tribal invasions, presumably by those tribes responsible for the end of the Bactrian kingdom. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Indo-Scythians" title="Indo-Scythians">Indo-Scythian</a> kingdom seems to have gradually pushed the remaining Indo-Greek kingdom towards the east. The Indo-Greek kingdom appears to have lingered on in western Punjab until about 10 AD, at which time it was finally ended by the Indo-Scythians.\n</p><p>After conquering the Indo-Greeks, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushan_empire" title="Kushan empire">Kushan empire</a> took over <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a>, the Greek language, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_script" title="Greek script">Greek script</a>, Greek coinage and artistic styles. Greeks continued being an important part of the cultural world of India for generations. The depictions of the Buddha appear to have been influenced by Greek culture: Buddha representations in the Ghandara period often showed Buddha under the protection of Herakles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanujit_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanujit-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several references in Indian literature praise the knowledge of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yavanas" title="Yavanas">Yavanas</a> or the Greeks. The <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a> compliments them as "the all-knowing Yavanas" (sarvajnaa yavanaa); e.g., "The Yavanas, O king, are all-knowing; the Suras are particularly so. The <a href="/wiki/Mleccha" title="Mleccha">mlecchas</a> are wedded to the creations of their own fancy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> such as flying machines that are generally called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vimanas" title="Vimanas">vimanas</a>. The "Brihat-Samhita" of the mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varahamihira" title="Varahamihira">Varahamihira</a> says: "The <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, though impure, must be honored since they were trained in sciences and therein, excelled others....." .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_states_and_Hellenistic_influences">Other states and Hellenistic influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Other states and Hellenistic influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scythian_comb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="357" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Scythian_comb.jpg/200px-Scythian_comb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Scythian_comb.jpg/300px-Scythian_comb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/15/Scythian_comb.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scythian_comb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greco-Scythian golden comb, from <a href="/wiki/Solokha" title="Solokha">Solokha</a>, early 4th century, <a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Hellenistic culture was at its height of world influence in the Hellenistic period. Hellenism or at least <a href="/wiki/Philhellenism" title="Philhellenism">Philhellenism</a> reached most regions on the frontiers of the Hellenistic kingdoms. Though some of these regions were not ruled by Greeks or even Greek speaking elites, certain Hellenistic influences can be seen in the historical record and <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> of these regions. Other regions had established contact with Greek colonies before this period, and simply saw a continued process of <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a> and intermixing.\n</p><p>Before the Hellenistic period, Greek colonies had been established on the coast of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean" title="Crimean">Crimean</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taman_peninsula" title="Taman peninsula">Taman peninsulas</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bosporan_Kingdom" title="Bosporan Kingdom">Bosporan Kingdom</a> was a multi-ethnic kingdom of Greek city states and local tribal peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Maeotians" title="Maeotians">Maeotians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracians</a>, Crimean <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spartocids" title="Spartocids">Spartocid dynasty</a> (438&#8211;110 BC). The Spartocids were a hellenized Thracian family from <a href="/wiki/Panticapaeum" title="Panticapaeum">Panticapaeum</a>. The Bosporans had long lasting trade contacts with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scythian" title="Scythian">Scythian</a> peoples of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontic-Caspian_steppe" title="Pontic-Caspian steppe">Pontic-Caspian steppe</a>, and Hellenistic influence can be seen in the Scythian settlements of the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Scythian_Neapolis" title="Scythian Neapolis">Scythian Neapolis</a>.
