Scythian pressure on the Bosporan kingdom under Paerisades V led to its eventual vassalage under the Pontic king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithradates_VI" title="Mithradates VI">Mithradates VI</a> for protection, c. 107 BC. It later became a Roman client state. Other <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> on the steppes of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> came into contact with Hellenistic culture through the Greeks of Bactria. Many Scythian elites purchased Greek products and some <a href="/wiki/Scythian_art" title="Scythian art">Scythian art</a> shows Greek influences. At least some Scythians seem to have become Hellenized, because we know of conflicts between the elites of the Scythian kingdom over the adoption of Greek ways. These Hellenized Scythians were known as the "young Scythians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The peoples around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olbia,_Ukraine" title="Olbia, Ukraine">Pontic Olbia</a>, known as the <i>Callipidae</i>, were intermixed and Hellenized Greco-Scythians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colchis-Nike.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="383" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Colchis-Nike.jpg/200px-Colchis-Nike.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Colchis-Nike.jpg/300px-Colchis-Nike.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Colchis-Nike.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colchis-Nike.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statuette of <a href="/wiki/Nike_(mythology)" title="Nike (mythology)">Nike</a>, Greek goddess of victory, from <a href="/wiki/Vani" title="Vani">Vani</a>, Georgia (country)</div></div></div>\n<p>The Greek colonies on the west coast of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_sea" title="Black sea">Black sea</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Histria_(ancient_city)" title="Histria (ancient city)">Istros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constan%C8%9Ba" title="Constan&#539;a">Tomi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mangalia" title="Mangalia">Callatis</a> traded with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thracian" title="Thracian">Thracian</a> <a href="/wiki/Getae" title="Getae">Getae</a> who occupied modern-day <a href="/wiki/Dobruja" title="Dobruja">Dobruja</a>. From the 6th century BC on, the multiethnic people in this region gradually intermixed with each other, creating a Greco-Getic populace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Numismatic evidence shows that Hellenic influence penetrated further inland. <a href="/wiki/Getae" title="Getae">Getae</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a> coined Getic <a href="/wiki/Tetradrachm" title="Tetradrachm">tetradrachms</a>, Getic imitations of Macedonian coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgian</a> kingdoms had trade relations with the Greek city-states on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> coast such as <a href="/wiki/Poti" title="Poti">Poti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sukhumi" title="Sukhumi">Sukhumi</a>. The kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a>, which later became a Roman client state, received Hellenistic influences from the Black Sea Greek colonies.\n</p><p>In Arabia, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, which was referred to by the Greeks as <a href="/wiki/Tylos" title="Tylos">Tylos</a>, the centre of pearl trading, when <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a> came to discover it serving under <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsen_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larsen-94">[94]</a></sup> The Greek admiral <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a> is believed to have been the first of Alexander\'s commanders to visit these islands. It is not known whether Bahrain was part of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, although the archaeological site at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qalat_Al_Bahrain" title="Qalat Al Bahrain">Qalat Al Bahrain</a> has been proposed as a Seleucid base in the Persian Gulf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Alexander had planned to settle the eastern shores of the Persian Gulf with Greek colonists, and although it is not clear that this happened on the scale he envisaged, Tylos was very much part of the Hellenised world: the language of the upper classes was Greek (although Aramaic was in everyday use), while Zeus was worshipped in the form of the Arabian sun-god Shams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Tylos even became the site of Greek athletic contests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="648" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG/200px-Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG/300px-Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG/400px-Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthaginian_hoplite_-_Oplita_cartaginese.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carthaginian hoplite (Sacred Band, end of the 4th century BC)</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> colony on the coast of <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. Carthaginian culture came into contact with the Greeks through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punic" title="Punic">Punic</a> colonies in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and through their widespread Mediterranean trade network. While the Carthaginians retained their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punic" title="Punic">Punic</a> culture and language, they did adopt some Hellenistic ways, one of the most prominent of which was their military practices. In 550 BC, <a href="/wiki/Mago_I_of_Carthage" title="Mago I of Carthage">Mago I of Carthage</a> began a series of military reforms which included copying the army of <a href="/wiki/Timoleon" title="Timoleon">Timoleon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrant_of_Syracuse" title="Tyrant of Syracuse">Tyrant of Syracuse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The core of Carthage\'s military was the Greek-style <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">phalanx</a> formed by citizen <a href="/wiki/Hoplite" title="Hoplite">hoplite</a> spearmen who had been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscripted" title="Conscripted">conscripted</a> into service, though their armies also included large numbers of mercenaries. After their defeat in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>, Carthage hired a Spartan mercenary captain, <a href="/wiki/Xanthippus_of_Carthage" title="Xanthippus of Carthage">Xanthippus of Carthage</a>, to reform their military forces. Xanthippus reformed the Carthaginian military along <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_army" title="Ancient Macedonian army">Macedonian army</a> lines.\n</p><p>By the 2nd century BC, the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a> also began to see Hellenistic culture influence its art and architecture. The Numidian royal monument at <a href="/wiki/Chemtou" title="Chemtou">Chemtou</a> is one example of Numidian Hellenized architecture. Reliefs on the monument also show the Numidians had adopted Greco-Macedonian type armor and shields for their soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ptolemaic Egypt was the center of Hellenistic influence in Africa and Greek colonies also thrived in the region of <a href="/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya" title="Cyrene, Libya">Cyrene, Libya</a>. The kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB" title="Mero&#235;">Mero&#235;</a> was in constant contact with Ptolemaic Egypt and Hellenistic influences can be seen in their art and archaeology. There was a temple to <a href="/wiki/Serapis" title="Serapis">Serapis</a>, the Greco-Egyptian god.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_Rome">Rise of Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Rise of Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_100bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1903" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/East-Hem_100bc.jpg/300px-East-Hem_100bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/East-Hem_100bc.jpg/450px-East-Hem_100bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/East-Hem_100bc.jpg/600px-East-Hem_100bc.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_100bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eastern hemisphere at the end of the 2nd century BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>Widespread Roman interference in the Greek world was probably inevitable given the general manner of the ascendancy of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>. This Roman-Greek interaction began as a consequence of the Greek city-states located along the coast of southern Italy. Rome had come to dominate the Italian peninsula, and desired the submission of the Greek cities to its rule. Although they initially resisted, allying themselves with <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus of Epirus</a>, and defeating the Romans at several battles, the Greek cities were unable to maintain this position and were absorbed by the Roman republic. Shortly afterwards, Rome became involved in Sicily, fighting against the <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginians</a> in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>. The end result was the complete conquest of Sicily, including its previously powerful Greek cities, by the Romans.\n</p><p>Roman entanglement in the Balkans began when Illyrian piratical raids on Roman merchants led to invasions of Illyria (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Illyrian_War" title="First Illyrian War">First</a> and, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Illyrian_War" title="Second Illyrian War">Second Illyrian Wars</a>). Tension between Macedon and Rome increased when the young king of Macedon, <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V</a>, harbored one of the chief pirates, <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_of_Pharos" title="Demetrius of Pharos">Demetrius of Pharos</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> (a former client of Rome). As a result, in an attempt to reduce Roman influence in the Balkans, Philip allied himself with Carthage after <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> had dealt the Romans a massive defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">Battle of Cannae</a> (216 BC) during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>. Forcing the Romans to fight on another front when they were at a nadir of manpower gained Philip the lasting enmity of the Romans&#8212;the only real result from the somewhat insubstantial <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">First Macedonian War</a> (215&#8211;202 BC).\n</p><p>Once the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a> had been resolved, and the Romans had begun to regather their strength, they looked to re-assert their influence in the Balkans, and to curb the expansion of Philip. A pretext for war was provided by Philip\'s refusal to end his <a href="/wiki/Cretan_War_(205%E2%80%93200_BC)" title="Cretan War (205&#8211;200 BC)">war</a> with <a href="/wiki/Attalid_dynasty" title="Attalid dynasty">Attalid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pergamum" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, both Roman allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The Romans, also allied with the <a href="/wiki/Aetolian_League" title="Aetolian League">Aetolian League</a> of Greek city-states (which resented Philip\'s power), thus declared war on Macedon in 200 BC, starting the <a href="/wiki/Second_Macedonian_War" title="Second Macedonian War">Second Macedonian War</a>. This ended with a decisive Roman victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cynoscephalae" title="Battle of Cynoscephalae">Battle of Cynoscephalae</a> (197 BC). Like most Roman peace treaties of the period, the resultant \'Peace of Flaminius\' was designed utterly to crush the power of the defeated party; a massive indemnity was levied, Philip\'s fleet was surrendered to Rome, and Macedon was effectively returned to its ancient boundaries, losing influence over the city-states of southern Greece, and land in Thrace and Asia Minor. The result was the end of Macedon as a major power in the Mediterranean.\n</p><p>As a result of the confusion in Greece at the end of the Second Macedonian War, the Seleucid Empire also became entangled with the Romans. The Seleucid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_III" title="Antiochus III">Antiochus III</a> had allied with Philip V of Macedon in 203 BC, agreeing that they should jointly conquer the lands of the boy-king of Egypt, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_V" title="Ptolemy V">Ptolemy V</a>. After defeating Ptolemy in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_Syrian_War" title="Fifth Syrian War">Fifth Syrian War</a>, Antiochus concentrated on occupying the Ptolemaic possessions in Asia Minor. However, this brought Antiochus into conflict with Rhodes and Pergamum, two important Roman allies, and began a \'cold war\' between Rome and Antiochus (not helped by the presence of Hannibal at the Seleucid court).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">[4]</a></sup> Meanwhile, in mainland Greece, the <a href="/wiki/Aetolian_League" title="Aetolian League">Aetolian League</a>, which had sided with Rome against Macedon, now grew to resent the Roman presence in Greece. This presented Antiochus III with a pretext to invade Greece and \'liberate\' it from Roman influence, thus starting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman-Syrian_War" title="Roman-Syrian War">Roman-Syrian War</a> (192&#8211;188 BC). In 191 BC, the Romans under Manius Acilius Glabrio routed him at Thermopylae and obliged him to withdraw to Asia. During the course of this war Roman troops moved into Asia for the first time, where they defeated Antiochus again at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magnesia" title="Battle of Magnesia">Battle of Magnesia</a> (190 BC). A crippling treaty was imposed on Antiochus, with Seleucid possessions in Asia Minor removed and given to Rhodes and Pergamum, the size of the Seleucid navy reduced, and a massive war indemnity invoked.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1411" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg/250px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg/375px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg/500px-Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Pierre_Peyron_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Perseus of Macedon surrenders to Paullus. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Pierre_Peyron" title="Jean-Fran&#231;ois Pierre Peyron">Jean-Fran&#231;ois Pierre Peyron</a> from 1802. <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Fine_Arts_(Budapest)" title="Museum of Fine Arts (Budapest)">Museum of Fine Arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Thus, in less than twenty years, Rome had destroyed the power of one of the successor states, crippled another, and firmly entrenched its influence over Greece. This was primarily a result of the over-ambition of the Macedonian kings, and their unintended provocation of Rome, though Rome was quick to exploit the situation. In another twenty years, the Macedonian kingdom was no more. Seeking to re-assert Macedonian power and Greek independence, Philip V\'s son <a href="/wiki/Perseus_of_Macedon" title="Perseus of Macedon">Perseus</a> incurred the wrath of the Romans, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Macedonian_War" title="Third Macedonian War">Third Macedonian War</a> (171&#8211;168 BC). Victorious, the Romans abolished the Macedonian kingdom, replacing it with four puppet republics; these lasted a further twenty years before Macedon was formally annexed as a Roman province (146 BC) after yet another rebellion under <a href="/wiki/Andriscus" title="Andriscus">Andriscus</a>. Rome now demanded that the Achaean League, the last stronghold of Greek independence, be dissolved. The Achaeans refused and declared war on Rome. Most of the Greek cities rallied to the Achaeans\' side, even slaves were freed to fight for Greek independence. The Roman consul <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Mummius" title="Lucius Mummius">Lucius Mummius</a> advanced from Macedonia and defeated the Greeks at <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a>, which was razed to the ground. In 146 BC, the Greek peninsula, though not the islands, became a Roman protectorate. Roman taxes were imposed, except in Athens and Sparta, and all the cities had to accept rule by Rome\'s local allies.\n</p><p>The Attalid dynasty of Pergamum lasted little longer; a Roman ally until the end, its final king <a href="/wiki/Attalus_III" title="Attalus III">Attalus III</a> died in 133 BC without an heir, and taking the alliance to its natural conclusion, willed Pergamum to the Roman Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-102">[102]</a></sup> The final Greek resistance came in 88 BC, when King <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_VI_of_Pontus" title="Mithridates VI of Pontus">Mithridates</a> <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">of Pontus</a> rebelled against Rome, captured Roman held Anatolia, and massacred up to 100,000 Romans and Roman allies across Asia Minor. Many Greek cities, including Athens, overthrew their Roman puppet rulers and joined him in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithridatic_wars" title="Mithridatic wars">Mithridatic wars</a>. When he was driven out of Greece by the Roman general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Lucius Cornelius Sulla</a>, the latter laid siege to Athens and razed the city. Mithridates was finally defeated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Pompeius_Magnus" title="Gnaeus Pompeius Magnus">Gnaeus Pompeius Magnus</a> (Pompey the Great) in 65 BC. Further ruin was brought to Greece by the Roman civil wars, which were partly fought in Greece. Finally, in 27 BC, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar_Augustus" title="Caesar Augustus">Augustus</a> directly annexed Greece to the new <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaea_Province,_Roman_Empire" title="Achaea Province, Roman Empire">province of Achaea</a>. The struggles with Rome had left Greece depopulated and demoralised. Nevertheless, Roman rule at least brought an end to warfare, and cities such as Athens, Corinth, Thessaloniki and <a href="/wiki/Patras" title="Patras">Patras</a> soon recovered their prosperity.\n</p><p>Contrarily, having so firmly entrenched themselves into Greek affairs, the Romans now completely ignored the rapidly disintegrating Seleucid empire (perhaps because it posed no threat); and left the Ptolemaic kingdom to decline quietly, while acting as a protector of sorts, in as much as to stop other powers taking Egypt over (including the famous line-in-the-sand <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Popillius_Laenas" title="Gaius Popillius Laenas">incident</a> when the Seleucid <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_IV_Epiphanes" title="Antiochus IV Epiphanes">Antiochus IV Epiphanes</a> tried to invade Egypt).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">[4]</a></sup> Eventually, instability in the near east resulting from the power vacuum left by the collapse of the Seleucid Empire caused the Roman <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompey_the_Great" title="Pompey the Great">Pompey the Great</a> to abolish the Seleucid rump state, absorbing much of Syria into the Roman Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-102">[102]</a></sup> Famously, the end of Ptolemaic Egypt came as the final act in the republican civil war between the Roman triumvirs <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Anthony</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_Caesar" title="Augustus Caesar">Augustus Caesar</a>. After the defeat of Anthony and his lover, the last Ptolemaic monarch, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Battle of Actium</a>, Augustus invaded Egypt and took it as his own personal fiefdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-102">[102]</a></sup> He thereby completed both the destruction of the Hellenistic kingdoms and the Roman Republic, and ended (in hindsight) the Hellenistic era.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancientlibraryalex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1090" data-file-width="1072" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ancientlibraryalex.jpg/220px-Ancientlibraryalex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ancientlibraryalex.jpg/330px-Ancientlibraryalex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ancientlibraryalex.jpg/440px-Ancientlibraryalex.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancientlibraryalex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a>, here shown in an artist\'s impression, was the largest and most significant <a href="/wiki/List_of_libraries_in_the_ancient_world" title="List of libraries in the ancient world">library of the ancient world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagan,_C._1980_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sagan,_C._1980-103">[103]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_Stone.JPG"><img alt=\'"A large dark grey-coloured slab of stone with text that uses Ancient Egyptian hieroglyphs, demotic and Greek script in three separate horizontal registers"\' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="3665" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Rosetta_Stone.JPG/220px-Rosetta_Stone.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Rosetta_Stone.JPG/330px-Rosetta_Stone.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Rosetta_Stone.JPG/440px-Rosetta_Stone.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rosetta_Stone.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_Stone" title="Rosetta Stone">Rosetta Stone</a>, a trilingual Ptolemaic decree establishing the religious cult of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_V" title="Ptolemy V">Ptolemy V</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In some fields Hellenistic culture thrived, particularly in its preservation of the past. The states of the Hellenistic period were deeply fixated with the past and its seemingly lost glories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The preservation of many classical and archaic works of art and literature (including the works of the three great classical tragedians, <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>) are due to the efforts of the Hellenistic Greeks. The museum and library of Alexandria was the center of this conservationist activity. With the support of royal stipends, Alexandrian scholars collected, translated, copied, classified, and critiqued every book they could find. Most of the great literary figures of the Hellenistic period studied at Alexandria and conducted research there. They were scholar poets, writing not only poetry but treatises on Homer and other archaic and classical Greek literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> retained its position as the most prestigious seat of higher education, especially in the domains of philosophy and rhetoric, with considerable libraries and philosophical schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleod_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcleod-106">[106]</a></sup> Alexandria had the monumental museum (i.e., research center) and <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a> which was estimated to have had 700,000 volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleod_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcleod-106">[106]</a></sup> The city of Pergamon also had a large library and became a major center of book production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleod_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-mcleod-106">[106]</a></sup> The island of Rhodes had a library and also boasted a famous finishing school for politics and diplomacy. Libraries were also present in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Kos</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> was educated in Athens and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> in Rhodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleod_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-mcleod-106">[106]</a></sup> Antioch was founded as a metropolis and center of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek learning</a> which retained its status into the era of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleod_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-mcleod-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> as the metropolis of the lower <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.\n</p><p>The spread of Greek culture and language throughout the Near East and Asia owed much to the development of newly founded cities and deliberate <a href="/wiki/Colonies_in_antiquity" title="Colonies in antiquity">colonization</a> policies by the successor states, which in turn was necessary for maintaining their military forces. Settlements such as <a href="/wiki/Ai-Khanoum" title="Ai-Khanoum">Ai-Khanoum</a>, on trade routes, allowed Greek culture to mix and spread. The language of Philip II\'s and Alexander\'s court and army (which was made up of various Greek and non-Greek speaking peoples) was a version of <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic Greek</a>, and over time this language developed into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koine" title="Koine">Koine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the successor states.\n</p><p>The identification of local gods with similar Greek deities, a practice termed \'<a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">Interpretatio graeca</a>\', stimulated the building of Greek-style temples, and Greek culture in the cities meant that buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(ancient_Greece)" title="Gymnasium (ancient Greece)">gymnasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Greece#Characteristics_of_the_buildings" title="Theatre of ancient Greece">theaters</a> became common. Many cities maintained nominal autonomy while under the rule of the local king or <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a>, and often had Greek-style institutions. Greek dedications, statues, architecture, and inscriptions have all been found. However, local cultures were not replaced, and mostly went on as before, but now with a new Greco-Macedonian or otherwise Hellenized elite. An example that shows the spread of Greek theater is <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>\'s story of the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crassus" title="Crassus">Crassus</a>, in which his head was taken to the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> court and used as a prop in a performance of <a href="/wiki/The_Bacchae" title="The Bacchae">The Bacchae</a>. Theaters have also been found: for example, in <a href="/wiki/Ai-Khanoum" title="Ai-Khanoum">Ai-Khanoum</a> on the edge of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, the theater has 35 rows &#8211; larger than the theater in <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>.\n</p><p>The spread of Greek influence and language is also shown through <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_coinage" title="Ancient Greek coinage">Ancient Greek coinage</a>. Portraits became more realistic, and the obverse of the coin was often used to display a propagandistic image, commemorating an event or displaying the image of a favored god. The use of Greek-style portraits and Greek language continued under the Roman, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthian_empire" title="Parthian empire">Parthian</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushan_empire" title="Kushan empire">Kushan empires</a>, even as the use of Greek was in decline.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenization_and_acculturation">Hellenization and acculturation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Hellenization and acculturation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="1746" decoding="async" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/220px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/330px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/440px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the first representations of the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, and an example of <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>, 1st-2nd century AD, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standing_Buddha_(Tokyo_National_Museum)" title="Standing Buddha (Tokyo National Museum)">Standing Buddha (Tokyo National Museum)</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1372" data-file-width="893" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg/220px-Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg/330px-Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg/440px-Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rampurva_bull_in_Presidential_Palace_high_closeup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bull <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capital</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rampurva" title="Rampurva">Rampurva</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Ashoka" title="Pillars of Ashoka">Pillars of Ashoka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a>, 3rd century BC. Located in the Presidential Palace of <a href="/wiki/Rashtrapati_Bhavan" title="Rashtrapati Bhavan">Rashtrapati Bhavan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>. The subject matter is Indian (<a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">zebu</a>), the global shape is influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> styles, and the floral band incorporates Hellenistic designs (<a href="/wiki/Flame_palmette" title="Flame palmette">flame palmettes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a></div>\n<p>The concept of Hellenization, meaning the adoption of Greek culture in non-Greek regions, has long been controversial. Undoubtedly Greek influence did spread through the Hellenistic realms, but to what extent, and whether this was a deliberate policy or mere cultural diffusion, have been hotly debated.\n</p><p>It seems likely that Alexander himself pursued policies which led to Hellenization, such as the foundations of new cities and Greek colonies. While it may have been a deliberate attempt to spread Greek culture (or as Arrian says, "to civilise the natives"), it is more likely that it was a series of pragmatic measures designed to aid in the rule of his enormous empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green,_p._21_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green,_p._21-108">[108]</a></sup> Cities and colonies were centers of administrative control and Macedonian power in a newly conquered region. Alexander also seems to have attempted to create a mixed Greco-Persian elite class as shown by the <a href="/wiki/Susa_weddings" title="Susa weddings">Susa weddings</a> and his adoption of some forms of Persian dress and court culture. He also brought Persian and other non-Greek peoples into his military and even the elite cavalry units of the <a href="/wiki/Companion_cavalry" title="Companion cavalry">companion cavalry</a>. Again, it is probably better to see these policies as a pragmatic response to the demands of ruling a large empire<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green,_p._21_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green,_p._21-108">[108]</a></sup> than to any idealized attempt to bringing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_culture" title="Greek culture">Greek culture</a> to the \'<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbarians" title="Barbarians">barbarians</a>\'. This approach was bitterly resented by the Macedonians and discarded by most of the Diadochi after Alexander\'s death. These policies can also be interpreted as the result of Alexander\'s possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalomania" title="Megalomania">megalomania</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green,_p._23_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green,_p._23-109">[109]</a></sup> during his later years.\n</p><p>After Alexander\'s death in 323 BC, the influx of Greek colonists into the new realms continued to spread Greek culture into Asia. The founding of new cities and military colonies continued to be a major part of the Successors\' struggle for control of any particular region, and these continued to be centers of cultural diffusion. The spread of Greek culture under the Successors seems mostly to have occurred with the spreading of Greeks themselves, rather than as an active policy.\n</p><p>Throughout the Hellenistic world, these Greco-Macedonian colonists considered themselves by and large superior to the native "barbarians" and excluded most non-Greeks from the upper echelons of courtly and government life. Most of the native population was not Hellenized, had little access to Greek culture and often found themselves discriminated against by their Hellenic overlords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnasiums" title="Gymnasiums">Gymnasiums</a> and their Greek education, for example, were for Greeks only. Greek cities and colonies may have exported Greek art and architecture as far as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus" title="Indus">Indus</a>, but these were mostly enclaves of Greek culture for the transplanted Greek <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a>. The degree of influence that Greek culture had throughout the Hellenistic kingdoms was therefore highly localized and based mostly on a few great cities like Alexandria and Antioch. Some natives did learn Greek and adopt Greek ways, but this was mostly limited to a few local elites who were allowed to retain their posts by the Diadochi and also to a small number of mid-level administrators who acted as intermediaries between the Greek speaking upper class and their subjects. In the Seleucid Empire, for example, this group amounted to only 2.5 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Official" title="Official">official</a> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a> nevertheless had a considerable influence on the cultures that had been affected by the Hellenistic expansion. As far as the Indian subcontinent, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_influence_on_Indian_art" title="Hellenistic influence on Indian art">Hellenistic influence on Indian art</a> was broad and far-reaching, and had effects for several centuries following the forays of Alexander the Great.\n</p><p>Despite their initial reluctance, the Successors seem to have later deliberately naturalized themselves to their different regions, presumably in order to help maintain control of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In the Ptolemaic kingdom, we find some Egyptianized Greeks by the 2nd century onwards. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_kingdom" title="Indo-Greek kingdom">Indo-Greek kingdom</a> we find kings who were converts to <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Menander_I" title="Menander I">Menander</a>). The Greeks in the regions therefore gradually become \'localized\', adopting local customs as appropriate. In this way, hybrid \'Hellenistic\' cultures naturally emerged, at least among the upper echelons of society.\n</p><p>The trends of Hellenization were therefore accompanied by Greeks adopting native ways over time, but this was widely varied by place and by social class. The farther away from the Mediterranean and the lower in social status, the more likely that a colonist was to adopt local ways, while the Greco-Macedonian elites and royal families usually remained thoroughly Greek and viewed most non-Greeks with disdain. It was not until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a> that a Ptolemaic ruler bothered to learn the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a> of their subjects.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_religion" title="Hellenistic religion">Hellenistic religion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zeus_Ammon_(Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1766" data-file-width="1464" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zeus_Ammon_%28Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen%29.jpg/200px-Zeus_Ammon_%28Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zeus_Ammon_%28Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen%29.jpg/300px-Zeus_Ammon_%28Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Zeus_Ammon_%28Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen%29.jpg/400px-Zeus_Ammon_%28Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zeus_Ammon_(Antikensammlung_M%C3%BCnchen).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Amun#Greece" title="Amun">Zeus-Ammon</a>, a deity with attributes from Greek and Egyptian gods.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the Hellenistic period, there was much continuity in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Greek religion</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_gods" title="Greek gods">Greek gods</a> continued to be worshiped, and the same rites were practiced as before. However the socio-political changes brought on by the conquest of the Persian empire and Greek emigration abroad meant that change also came to religious practices. This varied greatly by location. Athens, Sparta and most cities in the Greek mainland did not see much religious change or new gods (with the exception of the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a> in Athens),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> while the multi-ethnic Alexandria had a very varied group of gods and religious practices, including Egyptian, Jewish and Greek. Greek emigres brought their Greek religion everywhere they went, even as far as India and Afghanistan. Non-Greeks also had more freedom to travel and trade throughout the Mediterranean and in this period we can see Egyptian gods such as <a href="/wiki/Serapis" title="Serapis">Serapis</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Syria" title="Greater Syria">Syrian</a> gods <a href="/wiki/Atargatis" title="Atargatis">Atargatis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a>, as well as a Jewish <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a>, all coexisting on the island of <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a> alongside classical Greek deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> A common practice was to identify Greek gods with native gods that had similar characteristics and this created new fusions like Zeus-Ammon, <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> Hagne (a Hellenized <a href="/wiki/Atargatis" title="Atargatis">Atargatis</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a>-<a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>. Greek emigres faced individual religious choices they had not faced on their home cities, where the gods they worshiped were dictated by tradition.    \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="1784" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg/200px-Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg/300px-Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg/400px-Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cybele_Getty_Villa_57.AA.19.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cybele" title="Cybele">Cybele</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygian</a> mother Goddess, enthroned, with <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornucopia" title="Cornucopia">cornucopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mural_crown" title="Mural crown">Mural crown</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Hellenistic monarchies were closely associated with the religious life of the kingdoms they ruled. This had already been a feature of Macedonian kingship, which had priestly duties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Hellenestic kings adopted patron deities as protectors of their house and sometimes claimed descent from them. The Seleucids for example took on <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> as patron, the Antigonids had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herakles" title="Herakles">Herakles</a>, and the Ptolemies claimed <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a> among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The worship of dynastic ruler cults was also a feature of this period, most notably in Egypt, where the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Ptolemies</a> adopted earlier Pharaonic practice, and established themselves as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God-kings" title="God-kings">god-kings</a>. These cults were usually associated with a specific temple in honor of the ruler such as the <i>Ptolemaieia</i> at Alexandria and had their own festivals and theatrical performances. The setting up of ruler cults was more based on the systematized honors offered to the kings (sacrifice, <a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a>, statues, altars, hymns) which put them on par with the gods (<i>isotheism</i>) than on actual belief of their divine nature. According to Peter Green, these cults did not produce genuine belief of the divinity of rulers among the Greeks and Macedonians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The worship of Alexander was also popular, as in the long lived cult at <a href="/wiki/Erythrae" title="Erythrae">Erythrae</a> and of course, at Alexandria, where his tomb was located.\n</p><p>The Hellenistic age also saw a rise in the disillusionment with traditional religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The rise of philosophy and the sciences had removed the gods from many of their traditional domains such as their role in the movement of the heavenly bodies and natural disasters. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sophists" title="Sophists">Sophists</a> proclaimed the centrality of humanity and <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnosticism</a>; the belief in <a href="/wiki/Euhemerism" title="Euhemerism">Euhemerism</a> (the view that the gods were simply ancient kings and heroes), became popular. The popular philosopher <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> promoted a <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism#Religion" title="Epicureanism">view of disinterested gods</a> living far away from the human realm in <a href="/wiki/Metakosmia" title="Metakosmia">metakosmia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Apotheosis" title="Apotheosis">apotheosis</a> of rulers also brought the idea of divinity down to earth. While there does seem to have been a substantial decline in religiosity, this was mostly reserved for the educated classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_in_the_Greco-Roman_world" title="Magic in the Greco-Roman world">Magic</a> was practiced widely, and this, too, was a continuation from earlier times. Throughout the Hellenistic world, people would consult <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracles</a>, and use <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">charms</a> and figurines to deter misfortune or to cast spells. Also developed in this era was the complex system of <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_astrology" title="Hellenistic astrology">astrology</a>, which sought to determine a person\'s character and future in the movements of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">planets</a>. Astrology was widely associated with the cult of <a href="/wiki/Tyche" title="Tyche">Tyche</a> (luck, fortune), which grew in popularity during this period.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1622" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg/200px-Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg/300px-Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg/400px-Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relief_with_Menander_and_New_Comedy_Masks_-_Princeton_Art_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief with Menander and New Comedy Masks (Roman, AD 40-60) - the masks show three New Comedy stock characters: youth, false maiden, old man. <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University_Art_Museum" title="Princeton University Art Museum">Princeton University Art Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Hellenistic period saw the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Comedy" title="New Comedy">New Comedy</a>, the only few surviving representative texts being those of <a href="/wiki/Menander" title="Menander">Menander</a> (born 342/1 BC). Only one play, <i><a href="/wiki/Dyskolos" title="Dyskolos">Dyskolos</a></i>, survives in its entirety. The plots of this new Hellenistic <a href="/wiki/Comedy_of_manners" title="Comedy of manners">comedy of manners</a> were more domestic and formulaic, stereotypical low born characters such as slaves became more important, the language was colloquial and major motifs included <a href="/wiki/Escapism" title="Escapism">escapism</a>, marriage, romance and luck (<a href="/wiki/Tyche" title="Tyche">Tyche</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Though no Hellenistic tragedy remains intact, they were still widely produced during the period, yet it seems that there was no major breakthrough in style, remaining within the classical model. The <i>Supplementum Hellenisticum</i>, a modern collection of extant fragments, contains the fragments of 150 authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_poetry" title="Hellenistic poetry">Hellenistic poets</a> now sought patronage from kings, and wrote works in their honor. The scholars at the libraries in Alexandria and Pergamon focused on the collection, cataloging, and <a href="/wiki/Literary_criticism" title="Literary criticism">literary criticism</a> of classical Athenian works and ancient Greek myths. The poet-critic <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a>, a staunch elitist, wrote hymns equating Ptolemy II to Zeus and Apollo. He promoted short poetic forms such as the <a href="/wiki/Epigram" title="Epigram">epigram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epyllion" title="Epyllion">epyllion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iambus_(genre)" title="Iambus (genre)">iambic</a> and attacked epic as base and common ("big book, big evil" was his doctrine).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> He also wrote a massive catalog of the holdings of the library of Alexandria, the famous <a href="/wiki/Pinakes" title="Pinakes">Pinakes</a>. Callimachus was extremely influential in his time and also for the development of <a href="/wiki/Augustan_poetry" title="Augustan poetry">Augustan poetry</a>. Another poet, <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, attempted to revive the epic for the Hellenistic world with his <a href="/wiki/Argonautica" title="Argonautica">Argonautica</a>. He had been a student of Callimachus and later became chief librarian (<i>prostates</i>) of the library of Alexandria. Apollonius and Callimachus spent much of their careers feuding with each other. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_poetry" title="Pastoral poetry">Pastoral poetry</a> also thrived during the Hellenistic era, <a href="/wiki/Theocritus" title="Theocritus">Theocritus</a> was a major poet who popularized the genre.\n</p><p>This period also saw the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_novel" title="Ancient Greek novel">Ancient Greek novel</a>, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Daphnis_and_Chloe" title="Daphnis and Chloe">Daphnis and Chloe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ephesian_Tale" title="Ephesian Tale">Ephesian Tale</a>.\n</p><p>Around 240 BC Livius Andronicus, a Greek slave from southern Italy, translated Homer\'s <i>Odyssey</i> into Latin. Greek literature would have a dominant effect on the development of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin literature</a> of the Romans. The poetry of <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> were all based on Hellenistic styles.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_(Napoli,_1969)_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="867" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_%28Napoli%2C_1969%29_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg/180px-Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_%28Napoli%2C_1969%29_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_%28Napoli%2C_1969%29_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg/270px-Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_%28Napoli%2C_1969%29_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_%28Napoli%2C_1969%29_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg/360px-Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_%28Napoli%2C_1969%29_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paolo_Monti_-_Servizio_fotografico_(Napoli,_1969)_-_BEIC_6353768.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno of Citium</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoic</a> philosophy.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophy</a></div>\n<p>During the Hellenistic period, many different schools of thought developed. Athens, with its multiple philosophical schools, continued to remain the center of philosophical thought. However, Athens had now lost her political freedom, and Hellenistic philosophy is a reflection of this new difficult period. In this political climate, Hellenistic philosophers went in search of goals such as <a href="/wiki/Ataraxia" title="Ataraxia">ataraxia</a> (un-disturbedness), <a href="/wiki/Autarky" title="Autarky">autarky</a> (self-sufficiency) and <a href="/wiki/Apatheia" title="Apatheia">apatheia</a> (freedom from suffering), which would allow them to wrest well-being or <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a> out of the most difficult turns of fortune. This occupation with the inner life, with personal inner liberty and with the pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a> is what all Hellenistic philosophical schools have in common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicureans" title="Epicureans">Epicureans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(philosophy)" title="Cynicism (philosophy)">Cynics</a> rejected public offices and civic service, which amounted to a rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">polis</a> itself, the defining institution of the Greek world. <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> promoted <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a> based on freedom from pain as its ultimate goal. Cynics such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_of_Sinope" title="Diogenes of Sinope">Diogenes of Sinope</a> rejected all material possessions and social conventions (<i>nomos</i>) as unnatural and useless. The <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaics" title="Cyrenaics">Cyrenaics</a>, meanwhile, embraced <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">hedonism</a>, arguing that pleasure was the only true good. <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno of Citium</a>, taught that virtue was sufficient for <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a> as it would allow one to live in accordance with Nature or <a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">Logos</a>. Zeno became extremely popular; the Athenians set up a gold statue of him, and Antigonus II Gonatas invited him to the Macedonian court. The philosophical schools of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peripatetics" title="Peripatetics">Peripatetics</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(Classical)" title="Lyceum (Classical)">Lyceum</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> (<a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonism</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Academy</a>) also remained influential. The academy would eventually turn to <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">Academic Skepticism</a> under <a href="/wiki/Arcesilaus" title="Arcesilaus">Arcesilaus</a> until it was rejected by <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_of_Ascalon" title="Antiochus of Ascalon">Antiochus of Ascalon</a> (c. 90 BC) in favour of <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>. Hellenistic philosophy had a significant influence on the Greek ruling elite. Examples include Athenian statesman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_of_Phaleron" title="Demetrius of Phaleron">Demetrius of Phaleron</a>, who had studied in the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(Classical)" title="Lyceum (Classical)">lyceum</a>; the Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Cleomenes_III" title="Cleomenes III">Cleomenes III</a>, who was a student of the Stoic Sphairos of Borysthenes; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigonus_II" title="Antigonus II">Antigonus II</a>, who was also a well known Stoic. This can also be said of the Roman upper classes, where Stoicism was dominant, as seen in the <a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a> of the Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> and the works of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>.\n</p><p>The spread of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> throughout the Roman world, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">spread of Islam</a>, ushered in the end of Hellenistic philosophy and the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">Medieval philosophy</a> (often forcefully, as under <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I#Suppression_of_religions" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>), which was dominated by the three <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic</a> traditions: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy" title="Jewish philosophy">Jewish philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy" title="Early Islamic philosophy">early Islamic philosophy</a>. In spite of this shift, Hellenistic philosophy continued to influence these three religious traditions and the renaissance thought which followed them.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sciences">Sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Sciences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="871" data-file-width="626" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg/230px-Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg/345px-Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg/460px-Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg.png 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eratosthenes%27_method_for_determining_the_size_of_the_Earth.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eratosthenes\' method for determining the radius and circumference of the Earth.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P._Oxy._I_29.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1032" data-file-width="1694" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/P._Oxy._I_29.jpg/230px-P._Oxy._I_29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/P._Oxy._I_29.jpg/345px-P._Oxy._I_29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/P._Oxy._I_29.jpg/460px-P._Oxy._I_29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P._Oxy._I_29.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the oldest surviving fragments of Euclid\'s <i>Elements</i>, found at <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus" title="Oxyrhynchus">Oxyrhynchus</a> and dated to c. AD 100 (<a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Oxyrhynchus_29" title="Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 29">P. Oxy. 29</a>). The diagram accompanies Book II, Proposition 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_astronomy" title="Hellenistic astronomy">Hellenistic astronomy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_mathematics" title="Hellenistic mathematics">Hellenistic mathematics</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_geography" title="Hellenistic geography">Hellenistic geography</a></div>\n<p>Hellenistic culture produced seats of learning throughout the Mediterranean. Hellenistic science differed from Greek science in at least two ways: first, it benefited from the cross-fertilization of Greek ideas with those that had developed in the larger Hellenistic world; secondly, to some extent, it was supported by royal patrons in the kingdoms founded by Alexander\'s successors. Especially important to Hellenistic science was the city of Alexandria in Egypt, which became a major center of scientific research in the 3rd century BC. Hellenistic scholars frequently employed the principles developed in earlier Greek thought: the application of mathematics and deliberate empirical research, in their scientific investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hellenistic <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">Geometers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;287</span> &#8211; 212 BC), <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius of Perga</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;262</span>&#160;&#8211; c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;190</span> BC), and <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;325</span> &#8211; 265 BC), whose <i>Elements</i> became the most important textbook in <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> until the 19th century, built upon the work of the Hellenic era <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagoreans" title="Pythagoreans">Pythagoreans</a>. Euclid developed proofs for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pythagorean_Theorem" title="Pythagorean Theorem">Pythagorean Theorem</a>, for the infinitude of primes, and worked on the five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_solids" title="Platonic solids">Platonic solids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> used his knowledge of geometry to measure the <a href="/wiki/History_of_geodesy#Hellenic_world" title="History of geodesy">circumference of the Earth</a>. His calculation was remarkably accurate. He was also the first to calculate the <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt#Obliquity_of_the_ecliptic_(Earth\'s_axial_tilt)" title="Axial tilt">tilt of the Earth\'s axis</a> (again with remarkable accuracy). Additionally, he may have accurately calculated the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">distance from the Earth to the Sun</a> and invented the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leap_day" title="Leap day">leap day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Known as the "Father of Geography ", Eratosthenes also created the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_world_maps#Eratosthenes" title="Ancient world maps">map of the world</a> incorporating <a href="/wiki/Circle_of_latitude" title="Circle of latitude">parallels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">meridians</a>, based on the available geographical knowledge of the era.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="924" data-file-width="1036" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg/230px-NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg/345px-NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg/460px-NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Machine_d%27Anticyth%C3%A8re_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Antikythera_mechanism" title="Antikythera mechanism">Antikythera mechanism</a> was an ancient <a href="/wiki/Analog_computer" title="Analog computer">analog computer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washington_Post_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Post-129">[129]</a></sup> designed to calculate <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomical</a> positions.</div></div></div>\n<p>Astronomers like <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> (c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;190</span>&#160;&#8211; c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;120</span> BC) built upon the measurements of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_influence_on_Greek_astronomy#Influence_on_Hipparchus_and_Ptolemy" title="Babylonian influence on Greek astronomy">Babylonian</a> astronomers before him, to measure the <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> of the Earth. Pliny reports that Hipparchus produced the first systematic <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_astronomical_maps,_catalogs,_and_surveys" title="Timeline of astronomical maps, catalogs, and surveys">star catalog</a> after he observed a new star (it is uncertain whether this was a <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">nova</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>) and wished to preserve astronomical record of the stars, so that other new stars could be discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> It has recently been claimed that a celestial globe based on Hipparchus\'s star catalog sits atop the broad shoulders of a large 2nd-century Roman statue known as the <a href="/wiki/Farnese_Atlas" title="Farnese Atlas">Farnese Atlas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Another astronomer, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristarchos_of_Samos" title="Aristarchos of Samos">Aristarchos of Samos</a> developed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> system.\n</p><p>The level of Hellenistic achievement in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> is impressively shown by the <a href="/wiki/Antikythera_mechanism" title="Antikythera mechanism">Antikythera mechanism</a> (150&#8211;100 BC). It is a 37-gear mechanical computer which computed the motions of the Sun and Moon, including lunar and solar eclipses predicted on the basis of astronomical periods believed to have been learned from the <a href="/wiki/History_of_astronomy#Mesopotamia" title="History of astronomy">Babylonians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeth_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeth-132">[132]</a></sup> Devices of this sort are not found again until the 10th century, when a simpler eight-geared luni-solar calculator incorporated into an <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a> was described by the Persian scholar, <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (April 2010)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> Similarly complex devices were also developed by other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inventions_in_the_Muslim_world" title="Inventions in the Muslim world">Muslim engineers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">astronomers</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeth_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freeth-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2013)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">Medicine</a>, which was dominated by the Hippocratic tradition, saw new advances under <a href="/wiki/Praxagoras" title="Praxagoras">Praxagoras</a> of Kos, who theorized that blood traveled through the veins. <a href="/wiki/Herophilos" title="Herophilos">Herophilos</a> (335&#8211;280 BC) was the first to base his conclusions on dissection of the human body and animal vivisection, and to provide accurate descriptions of the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>, liver and other key organs. Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Philinus_of_Cos" title="Philinus of Cos">Philinus of Cos</a> (fl. 250), a student of Herophilos, a new medical sect emerged, the <a href="/wiki/Empiric_school" title="Empiric school">Empiric school</a>, which was based on strict observation and rejected unseen causes of the <a href="/wiki/Dogmatic_school" title="Dogmatic school">Dogmatic school</a>.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolos_of_Mendes" title="Bolos of Mendes">Bolos of Mendes</a> made developments in <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> was known for his work in plant classification. Krateuas wrote a compendium on botanic pharmacy. The library of Alexandria included a zoo for research and Hellenistic zoologists include Archelaos, Leonidas of Byzantion, Apollodoros of Alexandria and Bion of Soloi.\n</p><p>Technological developments from the Hellenistic period include cogged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gears" title="Gears">gears</a>, pulleys, the screw, <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes\' screw">Archimedes\' screw</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Screw_press" title="Screw press">screw press</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glassblowing" title="Glassblowing">glassblowing</a>, hollow bronze casting, surveying instruments, an odometer, the <a href="/wiki/Pantograph" title="Pantograph">pantograph</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clock</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Water_organ" title="Water organ">water organ</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Piston_pump" title="Piston pump">Piston pump</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The interpretation of Hellenistic science varies widely. At one extreme is the view of the English classical scholar Cornford, who believed that "all the most important and original work was done in the three centuries from 600 to 300 BC".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> At the other is the view of the Italian physicist and mathematician <a href="/wiki/Lucio_Russo" title="Lucio Russo">Lucio Russo</a>, who claims that scientific method was actually born in the 3rd century BC, to be forgotten during the Roman period and only revived in the Renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_science">Military science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Military science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_armies" title="Hellenistic armies">Hellenistic armies</a></div>\n<p>Hellenistic warfare was a continuation of the military developments of <a href="/wiki/Iphicrates" title="Iphicrates">Iphicrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>, particularly his use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Phalanx" title="Macedonian Phalanx">Macedonian Phalanx</a>, a dense formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalangite" title="Phalangite">pikemen</a>, in conjunction with heavy <a href="/wiki/Companion_cavalry" title="Companion cavalry">companion cavalry</a>.
