Armies of the Hellenistic period differed from those of the classical period in being largely made up of professional soldiers and also in their greater specialization and technical proficiency in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_warfare" title="Siege warfare">siege warfare</a>. Hellenistic armies were significantly larger than those of classical Greece relying increasingly on Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercenaries" title="Mercenaries">mercenaries</a> (<i>misthophoroi</i>; men-for-pay) and also on non-Greek soldiery such as Thracians, Galatians, Egyptians and Iranians. Some ethnic groups were known for their martial skill in a particular mode of combat and were highly sought after, including <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_armies#Tarantine_cavalry" title="Hellenistic armies">Tarantine cavalry</a>, Cretan archers, Rhodian slingers and Thracian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peltasts" title="Peltasts">peltasts</a>. This period also saw the adoption of new weapons and troop types such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thureophoroi" title="Thureophoroi">Thureophoroi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thorakitai" title="Thorakitai">Thorakitai</a> who used the oval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thureos" title="Thureos">Thureos</a> shield and fought with javelins and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machaira" title="Machaira">machaira</a> sword. The use of heavily armored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cataphracts" title="Cataphracts">cataphracts</a> and also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_archers" title="Horse archers">horse archers</a> was adopted by the Seleucids, Greco-Bactrians, Armenians and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">Pontus</a>. The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_elephants" title="War elephants">war elephants</a> also became common. Seleucus received Indian war elephants from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauryan_empire" title="Mauryan empire">Mauryan empire</a>, and used them to good effect at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ipsus" title="Battle of Ipsus">battle of Ipsus</a>. He kept a core of 500 of them at <a href="/wiki/Apamea_(Euphrates)" title="Apamea (Euphrates)">Apameia</a>. The Ptolemies used the smaller African elephant.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg/200px-Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg/300px-Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg/400px-Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Mechanical_Artillery._Pic_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient mechanical artillery: Catapults (standing), the chain drive of <a href="/wiki/Polybolos" title="Polybolos">Polybolos</a> (bottom center), <a href="/wiki/Gastraphetes" title="Gastraphetes">Gastraphetes</a> (on wall)</div></div></div>\n<p>Hellenistic military equipment was generally characterized by an increase in size. <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic-era_warships" title="Hellenistic-era warships">Hellenistic-era warships</a> grew from the <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">trireme</a> to include more banks of oars and larger numbers of rowers and soldiers as in the Quadrireme and Quinquereme. The Ptolemaic <a href="/wiki/Tessarakonteres" title="Tessarakonteres">Tessarakonteres</a> was the largest ship constructed in Antiquity. New siege engines were developed during this period. An unknown engineer developed the torsion-spring catapult (c. 360) and Dionysios of Alexandria designed a repeating <a href="/wiki/Ballista" title="Ballista">ballista</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Polybolos" title="Polybolos">Polybolos</a>. Preserved examples of ball projectiles range from 4.4&#160;kg to 78&#160;kg (or over 170&#160;lbs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demetrius_Poliorcetes" title="Demetrius Poliorcetes">Demetrius Poliorcetes</a> was notorious for the large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_engines" title="Siege engines">siege engines</a> employed in his campaigns, especially during the 12-month siege of Rhodes when he had Epimachos of Athens build a massive 160 ton siege tower named <a href="/wiki/Helepolis" title="Helepolis">Helepolis</a>, filled with artillery.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2600" data-file-width="1960" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg/200px-Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg/300px-Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg/400px-Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_woman_BM_GR1852.3-27.9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head of an old woman, a good example of <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg/200px-Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg/300px-Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg/400px-Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statua_di_Amore_e_Psiche.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sculpture of <a href="/wiki/Cupid_and_Psyche" title="Cupid and Psyche">Cupid and Psyche</a>, an example of the sensualism of Hellenistic art. 2nd-century AD Roman copy of a 2nd-century BC Greek original.</div></div></div>\n<p>The term <i>Hellenistic</i> is a modern invention; the Hellenistic World not only included a huge area covering the whole of the Aegean, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a> focused on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poleis" title="Poleis">Poleis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, but also a huge time range. In artistic terms this means that there is huge variety which is often put under the heading of "Hellenistic Art" for convenience.\n</p><p>Hellenistic art saw a turn from the idealistic, perfected, calm and composed figures of classical Greek art to a style dominated by <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a> and the depiction of emotion (<a href="/wiki/Pathos" title="Pathos">pathos</a>) and character (<a href="/wiki/Ethos" title="Ethos">ethos</a>). The motif of deceptively realistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalism_(arts)" title="Naturalism (arts)">naturalism</a> in art (<i>aletheia</i>) is reflected in stories such as that of the painter <a href="/wiki/Zeuxis" title="Zeuxis">Zeuxis</a>, who was said to have painted grapes that seemed so real that birds came and pecked at them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_nude" title="Female nude">female nude</a> also became more popular as epitomized by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphrodite_of_Cnidos" title="Aphrodite of Cnidos">Aphrodite of Cnidos</a> of <a href="/wiki/Praxiteles" title="Praxiteles">Praxiteles</a> and art in general became more erotic (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Leda_and_the_Swan#Eroticism" title="Leda and the Swan">Leda and the Swan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scopas#Pothos" title="Scopas">Scopa\'s Pothos</a>). The dominant ideals of Hellenistic art were those of sensuality and passion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>\n</p><p>People of all ages and social statuses were depicted in the art of the Hellenistic age. Artists such as <a href="/wiki/Peiraikos" title="Peiraikos">Peiraikos</a> chose mundane and lower class subjects for his paintings. According to Pliny, "He painted barbers\' shops, cobblers\' stalls, asses, eatables and similar subjects, earning for himself the name of rhyparographos [painter of dirt/low things]. In these subjects he could give consummate pleasure, selling them for more than other artists received for their large pictures" (<a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a>, Book XXXV.112). Even barbarians, such as the <a href="/wiki/Galatia_(Roman_province)" title="Galatia (Roman province)">Galatians</a>, were depicted in heroic form, prefiguring the artistic theme of the <a href="/wiki/Noble_savage" title="Noble savage">noble savage</a>. The image of Alexander the Great was also an important artistic theme, and all of the diadochi had themselves depicted imitating Alexander\'s youthful look. A number of the best-known works of Greek sculpture belong to the Hellenistic period, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laoco%C3%B6n_and_his_Sons" title="Laoco&#246;n and his Sons">Laoco&#246;n and his Sons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus_de_Milo" title="Venus de Milo">Venus de Milo</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Winged_Victory_of_Samothrace" title="Winged Victory of Samothrace">Winged Victory of Samothrace</a>.\n</p><p>Developments in painting included experiments in <a href="/wiki/Chiaroscuro" title="Chiaroscuro">chiaroscuro</a> by <a href="/wiki/Zeuxis" title="Zeuxis">Zeuxis</a> and the development of <a href="/wiki/Landscape_painting" title="Landscape painting">landscape painting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Still_life" title="Still life">still life</a> painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Greek temples built during the Hellenistic period were generally larger than classical ones, such as the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">temple of Artemis</a> at Ephesus, the temple of <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>, and the temple of Apollo at Didyma (rebuilt by Seleucus in 300 BC). The royal palace (<i>basileion</i>) also came into its own during the Hellenistic period, the first extant example being the massive 4th-century villa of Cassander at <a href="/wiki/Vergina" title="Vergina">Vergina</a>.\n</p><p>This period also saw the first written works of <a href="/wiki/Art_history" title="Art history">art history</a> in the histories of <a href="/wiki/Duris_of_Samos" title="Duris of Samos">Duris of Samos</a> and Xenokrates of Athens, a sculptor and a historian of sculpture and painting.\n</p><p>There has been a trend in writing the history of this period to depict Hellenistic art as a decadent style, following the <i>Golden Age</i> of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Athens</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, after having described the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_sculpture" title="Greek sculpture">sculpture</a> of the classical period, says: <i>Cessavit deinde ars</i> ("then art disappeared").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The 18th century terms <i><a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a></i> have sometimes been applied to the art of this complex and individual period. The renewal of the historiographical approach as well as some recent discoveries, such as the tombs of <a href="/wiki/Vergina" title="Vergina">Vergina</a>, allow a better appreciation of this period\'s artistic richness.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_period_and_modern_culture">Hellenistic period and modern culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Hellenistic period and modern culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Decadence" title="Decadence">Decadence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneration" title="Degeneration">Degeneration</a></div>\n<p>The focus on the Hellenistic period over the course of the 19th century by scholars and historians has led to an issue common to the study of historical periods; historians see the period of focus as a mirror of the period in which they are living. Many 19th-century scholars contended that the Hellenistic period represented a cultural decline from the brilliance of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">classical Greece</a>. Though this comparison is now seen as unfair and meaningless, it has been noted that even commentators of the time saw the end of a cultural era which could not be matched again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> This may be inextricably linked with the nature of government. It has been noted by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> that after the establishment of the Athenian democracy:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><i>...the Athenians found themselves suddenly a great power. Not just in one field, but in everything they set their minds to...As subjects of a tyrant, what had they accomplished?...Held down like slaves they had shirked and slacked; once they had won their freedom, not a citizen but he could feel like he was labouring for himself"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></i></dd></dl>\n<p>Thus, with the decline of the Greek polis, and the establishment of monarchical states, the environment and social freedom in which to excel may have been reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green-144">[144]</a></sup> A parallel can be drawn with the productivity of the city states of Italy during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Renaissance" title="The Renaissance">the Renaissance</a>, and their subsequent decline under autocratic rulers.\n</p><p>However, <a href="/wiki/William_Woodthorpe_Tarn" title="William Woodthorpe Tarn">William Woodthorpe Tarn</a>, between <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and the heyday of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, focused on the issues of racial and cultural confrontation and the nature of colonial rule. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Rostovtzeff" title="Michael Rostovtzeff">Michael Rostovtzeff</a>, who fled the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_(1917)" title="Russian Revolution (1917)">Russian Revolution</a>, concentrated predominantly on the rise of the capitalist bourgeoisie in areas of Greek rule. <a href="/wiki/Arnaldo_Momigliano" title="Arnaldo Momigliano">Arnaldo Momigliano</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Jew" title="Italian Jew">Italian Jew</a> who wrote before and after the Second World War, studied the problem of mutual understanding between races in the conquered areas. <a href="/wiki/Moses_Hadas" title="Moses Hadas">Moses Hadas</a> portrayed an optimistic picture of synthesis of culture from the perspective of the 1950s, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_William_Walbank" title="Frank William Walbank">Frank William Walbank</a> in the 1960s and 1970s had a materialistic approach to the Hellenistic period, focusing mainly on class relations. Recently, however, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papyrologist" title="Papyrologist">papyrologist</a> C. Pr&#233;aux has concentrated predominantly on the economic system, interactions between kings and cities, and provides a generally pessimistic view on the period. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(historian)" title="Peter Green (historian)">Peter Green</a>, on the other hand, writes from the point of view of late-20th-century <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>, his focus being on <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a>, the breakdown of convention, experiments, and a postmodern disillusionment with all institutions and political processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hellenistic_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="24" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Parthenon_from_west.jpg/32px-Parthenon_from_west.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Parthenon_from_west.jpg/48px-Parthenon_from_west.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Parthenon_from_west.jpg/64px-Parthenon_from_west.jpg 2x" width="32"/></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Ancient_Greece" title="Portal:Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<div style="clear:none;float:left;width:30.0em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Ancient Carthage</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dehellenization" title="Dehellenization">Dehellenization</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenism_(Academia)" title="Hellenism (Academia)">Hellenism (Academia)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hellenism_(neoclassicism)" The senior posts in the civil service were also held by Greeks. Although the Ptolemies and the Seleucids were perpetual rivals, both dynasties were Greek and ruled by means of Greek officials and Greek soldiers. Both governments made great efforts to attract immigrants from Greece, thereby adding yet another racial element to the population.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Syria%2C+Lebanon%2C+Jordan&amp;rft.pages=34&amp;rft.pub=Thames+%26+Hudson&amp;rft.date=1967&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F585939&amp;rft.au=Glubb%2C+John+Bagot&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHellenistic+period"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bugh, Glenn R. The Seleucids respected the cultural and religious sensibilities of their subjects but preferred to rely on Greek or Macedonian soldiers and administrators for the day-to-day business of governing. The Greek population of the cities, reinforced until the second century BC by emigration from Greece, formed a dominant, although not especially cohesive, elite.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Western+civilization%3A+a+history+of+European+society&amp;rft.pages=76&amp;rft.pub=Thomson+Wadsworth&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-534-62164-3&amp;rft.au=Steven+C.+Hause&amp;rft.au=William+S.+Maltby&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHellenistic+period"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Victor,_Royce_M._2010_55-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Victor,_Royce_M._2010_55_60-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Victor, Royce M. These owe something to the pervasive influence of Achaemenid architecture and sculpture, with no little Greek architectural ornament and sculptural style as well.
